Imam Sanusi's Doctrine on Karamat
Imam Sanusi states his doctrine on karamat and critiques both innovators and some jurists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Sanusi's balanced position on karamat (miracles of saints) and his critique of both innovators and overly strict jurists.

Core Idea
Karamat are real and nearly match prophetic miracles; denying them is extreme.

Imam Sanusi addresses a sensitive topic: the miracles of saints (karamat). While some reject them entirely, others impose harsh conditions. Sanusi steers a middle path, affirming their reality while criticizing both innovators and those who condemn believers. This passage presents his core stance and a specific example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of Sanusi's argument.
Pay attention to the quote from Sharh al-Wusta.
2
Identify Sanusi's main doctrine
Notice that Sanusi aligns himself with the majority of verifiers from Ahl al-Sunnah. He affirms karamat and compares them to prophetic miracles.
He says karamat 'nearly match' mu'jizat.
3
Analyze his critique of deniers
Sanusi criticizes two groups: people of innovation (ahl al-bid'a) who deny karamat because they never experienced them, and some Sunni jurists who declared takfir over a specific karamah story.
The story of Ibrahim ibn Adham being in two places at once.
4
Reflect on his humble conclusion
Sanusi ends with humility, comparing himself to one on the shore of hope, unable to reach the depths of tawhid that the awliya have attained.
This shows his respect for the awliya and his own modesty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومذهب الإمام السنوسي في الكرامة ووقوعها مذهب عامة المحققين من أهل السنة والجماعة ، وقد نثر الحديث عنها مختصراً تارة ومطولاً أخرى في « سنوسياته ( المباركة ، وله عبارة ذهبية نقدية لم تحجم قدرة الله تعالى ولم تقصر من شأن الكرامة في «شرح الوسطى » إذ قال فيها : ) فكرامات الأولياء باعتبار ظهورها تكاد تلحق بمعجزات الأنبياء . وإنكارها ليس بعجيب من أهل البدع والأهواء ؛ إذ لم يشاهدوا ذلك من أنفسهم قطُّ ( إلى أن قال : ( وإنما العجب من بعض فقهاء أهل السنة ؛ حيث قال فيما روي عن إبراهيم بن أدهم أنهم رأوه بالبصرة يوم التروية وفي ذلك اليوم بمكة : إنَّ من اعتقد جواز ذلك يكفر !! )(۲) ولك أن تتأمَّل كلمته المتواضعة المنكسرة حينما حكى شروط الولي التي ذكرها الإمام ابن دهاق ؛ إذ قال : ( ونحن بالنسبة إلى هذا المقام مقام أولياء الله تعالى وخاصة حضرته . . على ساحل التمني نغترف من بحر التوحيد والعرفان الذي خاضوا لججه ، وغابوا فيه
And the doctrine of Imam al-Sanusi regarding karamah and its occurrence is the doctrine of the generality of the verifiers (muhaqqiqīn) from Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama‘ah. He has scattered the discussion about it, sometimes briefly and sometimes at length, in his blessed ‘Sanusiyyat’. He has a golden critical statement in his ‘Sharh al-Wusta’ that does not diminish the power of Allah nor belittle the status of karamah. He said in it: 'Verily, the karamāt of the awliyā’, in terms of their appearance, nearly match the miracles (mu‘jizāt) of the prophets. Denying them is not surprising from the people of innovation and desires, for they have never experienced such from themselves at all.' Up to where he said: 'But the wonder is from some jurists of Ahl al-Sunnah who said, regarding what was narrated about Ibrahim ibn Adham that they saw him in Basra on the day of Tarwiyah and on that same day in Makkah: “Whoever believes that such is permissible becomes a disbeliever!!”' And you may ponder his humble, broken statement when he related the conditions of the wali that Imam Ibn Dihaq mentioned, as he said: 'We, in relation to this station — the station of the awliyā’ of Allah and the special ones of His Presence… are on the shore of hope; we scoop from the sea of tawḥīd and ‘irfān in which they plunged its depths and were lost.'
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Sanusi's theological position on karamat (miracles of saints). It states that his doctrine is that of the majority of verifiers among Ahl al-Sunnah. He has discussed it in his works, and in his commentary 'Sharh al-Wusta' he makes a critical statement: He affirms that the karamat of the awliya, in their apparent form, are nearly on par with the miracles of the prophets. Denial of karamat is not surprising from people of innovation (ahl al-bid‘a) because they have never witnessed such things themselves. However, he expresses amazement at some jurists from Ahl al-Sunnah who declared takfir (disbelief) on those who believe the story of Ibrahim ibn Adham being in Basra and Mecca on the same day — a famous karamah. The Imam finds this extreme. Then he humbly states that in relation to the high station of the awliya, we are merely on the shore of hope, scooping from the sea of tawhid and irfan that they have fully immersed in. This lesson shows the balance: affirming karamat without exaggeration, and critiquing both innovators and overly strict Sunnis.

Key Terms
كرامة
معجزة
أهل البدع
تكفير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Karamat are impossible or lead to shirk
Correction: Sanusi affirms karamat are real and nearly match prophetic miracles; denying them is extreme.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Sanusi say about the karamat of awliya compared to miracles of prophets?
A) They are exactly the same
B) They are lesser and insignificant
C) They nearly match in terms of appearance
D) They are only spiritual, not physical

Key Takeaway
Imam Sanusi affirms karamat as real phenomena that nearly match prophetic miracles, and criticizes both those who deny them and those who declare disbelief for believing in specific karamat stories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear stories of extraordinary favors granted to saints, does your heart naturally incline to believe or to dismiss? What does that reaction reveal about the state of your iman? This lesson challenges you to affirm that Allah can honor whom He wills—will you ask Him for a greater share of nearness?
?
Imam Sanusi humbly places himself on the 'shore of hope' while acknowledging the awliya are lost in the sea of tawhid. Do you truly long to be lost in that sea, or are you content with shallow faith? What one concrete step will you take today to move from the shore toward the deep waters of divine knowledge?
?
The jurists who declared disbelief for a karamah story lacked humility before Allah's power. Where in your own life might you be imposing limits on what Allah can do for His righteous servants, and what does that say about your awe of Him? Ask Allah to expand your heart to believe in His unlimited generosity.

The Three Schools on Voluntary Actions of Living Beings
Sanusi enumerates the three schools on voluntary actions and defines each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinct positions of the Jabriyya, Qadariyya, and Ahl al-Sunna regarding the role of divine and created power in voluntary actions.

Core Idea
The three schools differ on whether actions occur by eternal power alone, created power alone, or both with no effect from created power.

Sanusi introduces the three theological schools that address a fundamental question: who or what is the true agent of voluntary actions? Before diving into the proofs, he clearly defines each school's stance on the relationship between God's eternal power and the creature's temporally originating power. This passage sets the stage for the subsequent discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Note that the text is incomplete; it cuts off at 'على سبيل'.
The passage is the opening of the second introduction.
2
Identify the three schools
The passage presents three schools: Jabriyya, Qadariyya, and Ahl al-Sunna. Find each definition and note the keywords: القدرة الأزلية (eternal power), القدرة الحادثة (temporally originating power).
3
Understand key terms
Note the terms 'مباشرة' (directly) and 'تولدا' (by generation) for the Qadariyya, and 'اضطرار' (compulsory) for the Jabriyya. The Ahl al-Sunna use 'مقارنة' (accompaniment) and 'لا تأثير لها' (no effect).
These terms are central to the debate.
4
Grasp the Ahl al-Sunna position
Ahl al-Sunna affirm all actions occur by eternal power alone, but voluntary actions are accompanied by a created power that has no effect, neither directly nor by generation.
This is the nuanced middle position.
5
Reflect on common misunderstanding
The common misunderstanding is that Ahl al-Sunna deny any role for created power. Actually, they affirm its accompaniment but deny its causal effect.
This is provided in the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية في المذاهِب في أفعَالِ الحَيَوانِ الاِختِيَارِيَّة السنة وَالْمَذَاهِبُ فِي الْأَفْعَالِ ثَلَاثَةٌ (1) مَذْهَبُ الْجَبْرِيَّةِ ، وَمَذْهَبُ الْقَدَرِيَّةِ ، وَمَذْهَبُ أَهْل فَمَذْهَبُ الْجَبْرِيَّةِ : وُجُودُ الْأَفْعَالِ كُلِّهَا بِالْقُدْرَةِ الْأَزَلِيَّةِ فَقَطْ مِنْ غَيْرِ مُقَارَنَةٍ لِقُدْرَةٍ حَادِثَةٍ . وَمَذْهَبُ الْقَدَرِيَّةِ : وُجُودُ الأَفْعَالِ الاخْتِيَارِيَّةِ بِالْقُدْرَةِ الْحَادِثَةِ فَقَطْ مُبَاشَرَةٌ أَوْ تَوَلُّداً . وَمَذْهَبُ أَهْلِ السُّنَّةِ : وُجُودُ الْأَفْعَالِ كُلِّهَا بِالْقُدْرَةِ الْأَزَلِيَّةِ فَقَطْ مَعَ مُقَارَنَةِ الأَفْعَالِ الِاخْتِيَارِيَّةِ لِقُدْرَةٍ حَادِثَةٍ لَا تَأْثِيرَ لَهَا لا مُبَاشَرَةً وَلا تَوَلُّداً يعني بالأفعالِ : أفعال الحيوانات عاقلةً أو غير عاقلة • فجعلَ الجَبْريَّةُ جميعها اضطراراً (۱) ؛ كحركة الارتعاش ، ليسَ للحيوان قدرة تتعلق بها وجعلَ القدريَّةُ الاختياريَّة منها (۲) - وهو ما لا يُحس فيه الإلجاء إلى الفعل (۳) ـ مُخترعاً للحيوانِ بالقدرةِ التي خلق الله تعالى له على سبيل
The Second Introduction: On the schools regarding the voluntary actions of animals. The schools regarding actions are three: (1) the school of the Jabriyya, the school of the Qadariyya, and the school of Ahl [al-Sunna]. The school of the Jabriyya: the existence of all actions solely by the eternal power without the accompaniment of a temporally originating power. The school of the Qadariyya: the existence of voluntary actions solely by the temporally originating power, either directly or by generation. The school of Ahl al-Sunna: the existence of all actions solely by the eternal power, with the accompaniment of a temporally originating power for voluntary actions, which has no effect, neither directly nor by generation. He means by 'actions': the actions of animals, rational or non-rational. So the Jabriyya made all of them compulsory, like the movement of tremor; the animal has no power that pertains to them. And the Qadariyya made the voluntary ones among them—which is what does not feel compulsion to the act—as invented by the animal by the power that Allah the Exalted has created for it, by way of...
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of the three main Islamic theological schools regarding the nature of voluntary actions of living beings (animals). The author begins by introducing the second preamble (al-Muqaddima al-Thāniya) on this topic. He states that there are three schools: Jabriyya, Qadariyya, and Ahl al-Sunna. The Jabriyya hold that all actions occur solely by God's eternal power without any accompaniment of a created power in the creature. The Qadariyya hold that voluntary actions exist solely by the creature's temporally originating power, either directly (mubāshara) or by generation (tawallud). Ahl al-Sunna affirm that all actions exist solely by God's eternal power, but that voluntary actions are accompanied by a created power that has no effect, whether directly or by generation. The author then clarifies that 'actions' here refers to the actions of animals, rational or non-rational. He elaborates that the Jabriyya consider all actions compulsory (idtirār), like the involuntary movement of tremor, with no power in the animal to affect them. The Qadariyya consider the voluntary actions—defined as those where no compulsion is felt—as invented (mukhtara‘) by the animal through the power Allah created in it, by way of [the text cuts off]. The point is to delineate the precise positions of each school on the role of divine and human agency.

Key Terms
الجبرية
القدرية
أهل السنة
القدرة الأزلية
القدرة الحادثة
مباشرة
تولدا
اضطرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ahl al-Sunna deny any role for created power in voluntary actions.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna affirm that created power accompanies voluntary actions, but it has no causal effect (neither directly nor by generation).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of Ahl al-Sunna regarding the relationship between eternal power and created power in voluntary actions?
A) Both powers are effective independently.
B) Created power has no effect, but it accompanies the action.
C) Created power is the sole cause of voluntary actions.
D) Eternal power is not involved in voluntary actions.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna affirm that all actions occur by God's power alone, yet voluntary actions are accompanied by a created power that has no causal effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your daily actions—your prayers, your sins, your good deeds—do you truly believe that your own power has no effect? How does that truth change how you ask Allah for help and how you take responsibility for your choices? What would it cost you to live consistently with this belief?
?
If all actions exist by God's power alone, then your gratitude for a good deed cannot be pride in your own strength. Look at your last truly good deed: can you thank Allah for it without feeling you contributed anything? If not, what does that reveal about your heart's attachment to your own ability?
?
The Jabriyya view removes blame but also removes motivation. Examine your life: have you ever used the belief in predestination as an excuse for laziness or sin? What would change if you fully embraced the Ahl al-Sunna position—that your power is real but ineffective, and you are still commanded to strive? Will you act on that today?

The Meaning and Controversy of 'Qadariyya'
A footnote explaining the origin and meaning of the term 'Qadariyya'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the origin and meaning of the term 'Qadariyya' and why early theologians considered it a misapplied label.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya are named for affirming their own power, not for denying God's decree.

This footnote from al-Azharī's lexicon clarifies the controversial label 'Qadariyya'. The term was used to brand a group accused of denying divine destiny, but some of them rejected the label, claiming it should apply to those who affirm God's decree. As we'll see, the author exposes this as a rhetorical trick.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the key terms such as 'نبز' (stigma) and 'تمويه' (obfuscation).
The passage is a footnote, so it is dense with layered meaning.
2
Identify the two claims
Notice how the passage first presents the common definition from al-Azharī, then reports the Qadariyya's own counter-argument that the label does not apply to them.
3
Analyze the refutation
See how the author dismisses their defense as obfuscation by pointing out that they affirm destiny for themselves, hence the name fits.
This is the crucial point: the label is based on their own doctrine of self-determination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲) القدرية : قال العلامة الأزهري في تهذيب اللغة ) (۳۷/۹ ) : ( قوم ينسبون إلى التكذيب بما قدَّرَ الله من الأشياء ، وقال بعض متكلميهم : لا يلزمنا هذا النبز ؛ لأنَّا ننفي القدر عن الله ، ومن أثبته فهو أولى به ، وهذا تمويه منهم لأنهم يثبتون القدر لأنفسهم ، ولذلك سُمُّوا قدرية )
(2) al-Qadariyya: al-‘Allāma al-Azharī said in Tahdhīb al-Lugha (37/9): 'A people who attribute to denying what Allah has decreed of things.' And some of their theologians said: 'This branding does not apply to us because we deny destiny (al-qadr) from Allah, and whoever affirms it is more deserving of it.' And this is obfuscation from them, because they affirm destiny for themselves, and for that they were called Qadariyya.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This footnote clarifies the term 'Qadariyya' (the free-will school). The author quotes al-Azharī from his lexicon Tahdhīb al-Lugha, defining them as people who are accused of denying God's decree (qadr). He then reports a counterargument from some Qadariyya theologians: they claim the label does not apply to them because they deny that God has any role in destiny, and those who affirm divine destiny are more deserving of the name. The author dismisses this as obfuscation (tamwīh), pointing out that they affirm destiny for themselves (i.e., they assert their own power to determine actions), hence they are rightly called 'Qadariyya'.

Key Terms
القدرية
التكذيب
القدر
النبز
التمويه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qadariyya are so called because they deny God's qadr.
Correction: They are actually called Qadariyya because they affirm qadr for themselves.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the Qadariyya's defense against the label 'Qadariyya' as obfuscation?
A) Because they deny divine destiny entirely.
B) Because they affirm destiny for themselves, not for God.
C) Because they accept the name willingly.
D) Because they are not a real sect.

Key Takeaway
The term 'Qadariyya' is a label based on their own doctrine of self-determination, not on their denial of divine decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used clever wording to avoid a label that actually describes your belief? The Qadariyya said, 'This branding does not apply to us because we deny qadr from Allah.' Are you, like them, more concerned with avoiding a name than with the truth of what you hold?
?
The footnote calls their argument 'obfuscation.' Is there an area of your faith where you have twisted logic to escape correction? What would it cost you to accept the label and change your stance?
?
The name Qadariyya sticks because they affirm power for themselves. In your daily life, do you live as if you are the ultimate cause of outcomes? How does this lesson urge you to realign your self-attribution?

The Two Madhhabs on the Causation of Actions
Sanusi defines two theological positions on action causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key difference between the Qadariyya and Ahl al-Sunna on how voluntary actions come to exist.

Core Idea
Actions are either by human power alone or by divine power alone.

This passage presents two opposing views on the origin of voluntary actions. The Qadariyya attribute them entirely to human created power, while Ahl al-Sunna attribute them solely to the eternal divine power, with human power being a non-effecting accompaniment. Read on to see the exact formulations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Notice the parallel structure: first the Qadariyya position, then the Ahl al-Sunna position, then a clarifying remark.
The passage is short but packed with technical terms.
2
Contrast the two doctrines
Identify the key differences: Qadariyya says actions exist only by temporal power, directly or by generation. Ahl al-Sunna says all actions exist only by eternal power, with temporal power accompanying but having no effect.
Pay attention to 'only' (فقط) and 'accompanied by' (مَعَ).
3
Understand the key terms
Make sure you grasp the meaning of 'voluntary actions', 'temporal power', 'eternal power', 'directly', and 'by generation'. These are central to the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَذْهَبُ الْقَدَرِيَّةِ : وُجُودُ الأَفْعَالِ الاخْتِيَارِيَّةِ بِالْقُدْرَةِ الْحَادِثَةِ فَقَطْ مُبَاشَرَةٌ أَوْ تَوَلُّداً . وَمَذْهَبُ أَهْلِ السُّنَّةِ : وُجُودُ الْأَفْعَالِ كُلِّهَا بِالْقُدْرَةِ الْأَزَلِيَّةِ فَقَطْ مَعَ مُقَارَنَةِ الأَفْعَالِ الِاخْتِيَارِيَّةِ لِقُدْرَةٍ حَادِثَةٍ لَا تَأْثِيرَ لَهَا لا مُبَاشَرَةً وَلا تَوَلُّداً . يعني بالأفعالِ : أفعال الحيوانات عاقلةً أو غير عاقلة .
The doctrine of the Qadariyya: the existence of voluntary actions is only by the temporal power, directly or by generation. The doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna: the existence of all actions is only by the eternal power, with the voluntary actions accompanied by a temporal power that has no effect, neither directly nor by generation. He means by 'actions': the actions of animals, rational or non-rational.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a definition and comparison of two theological positions. It first defines Qadariyya belief: voluntary actions exist solely by the created power of the human agent, whether the action is directly produced or generated indirectly. Then defines Ahl al-Sunna belief: all actions exist solely by the eternal divine power, but voluntary actions occur alongside a created power in the human that does not actually cause the action, neither directly nor by generation. The author clarifies that 'actions' here refers to actions of all living beings, rational or not. This sets the stage for the detailed discussion of human power and divine agency.

Key Terms
الأَفْعَال الاخْتِيَارِيَّةِ
الْقُدْرَةِ الْحَادِثَةِ
الْقُدْرَةِ الْأَزَلِيَّةِ
مُبَاشَرَة
تَوَلُّدًا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ahl al-Sunna denies any human power in actions, leaving no role for human agency.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna affirms a temporal power that accompanies voluntary actions, but this power has no causal effect—it is a non-effecting accompaniment.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunna, what is the role of the temporal power in voluntary actions?
A) It creates the action
B) It is the sole cause
C) It accompanies but has no effect
D) It generates the action through causality

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna affirm that all actions are from Allah alone, while human power is a non-effectual accompaniment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you inwardly credit your own strength or Allah's grace? How does the Sunni position challenge your default attitude?
?
Think of a sin you committed recently. Did you view it as entirely your fault, or did you consider that Allah's power was involved in allowing it? How should this lesson reshape your repentance?
?
If all actions are from Allah alone, what genuine role does your will and effort play? Does this make you feel helpless or more reliant on Allah? Examine your heart honestly.

Jabriyya and Qadariyya on Human Power
Sanusi contrasts the determinism of Jabriyya with the independent agency claimed by Qadariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental differences between the Jabriyya and Qadariyya regarding the nature of human power and divine action.

Core Idea
Two extreme views on who creates human actions — the Jabriyya deny all human power, the Qadariyya overextend it.

Sanusi now presents two erroneous positions that were debated among early theologians. Understanding where they went wrong is essential for grasping the balanced Sunni position. This passage contrasts the Jabriyya's complete determinism with the Qadariyya's claim of independent human creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own contrast come through before you interpret.
Notice how Sanusi uses 'Jabriyya' and 'Qadariyya' as labels.
2
Identify the two claims
Identify what each group says about human actions and divine involvement. Jabriyya say all are compelled; Qadariyya distinguish voluntary and involuntary.
3
Examine the examples
Note the examples given: tremor (involuntary) for Jabriyya, and colors/tastes/smells for Qadariyya. Understand why these examples support each position.
4
Reflect on the boundary
Consider how each group draws the line between what Allah creates and what the human creates. Jabriyya give no role to humans; Qadariyya give too much.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فجعلَ الجَبْريَّةُ جميعها اضطراراً كحركة الارتعاش، ليسَ للحيوان قدرة تتعلق بها. وجعلَ القدريَّةُ الاختياريَّة منها - وهو ما لا يُحس فيه الإلجاء إلى الفعل - مُخترعاً للحيوانِ بالقدرةِ التي خلق الله تعالى له على سبيل الاستقلال، وليس للمولى تبارك وتعالى فيها اختراع عندهم، وإنَّما الذي يُوجِدُهُ سبحانه وتعالى فيهم ما لا يتيسر منها عليهم؛ كالألوان والطعوم والروائح وحركة الارتعاش ونحو ذلك.
Thus the Jabriyya made all of them (actions) compelled, like the movement of tremor, with no power for the animal pertaining to them. And the Qadariyya made the voluntary ones among them—that is, what does not feel compulsion to the act—an invention of the animal by the power that Allah created for him independently, and according to them, the Lord Most High has no invention in them, but rather what He causes to exist in them is what is not possible for them by themselves, such as colors, tastes, smells, the movement of tremor, and the like.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage explains the positions of two extreme sects. First, Jabriyya (determinists) claim all actions of animals are forced, like involuntary trembling, and the animal has no power at all over them. Second, Qadariyya claim that voluntary actions (those where no compulsion is felt) are independently created by the animal using the power Allah gave them, with Allah having no role in creating those actions. However, they admit that Allah creates in them things they cannot produce themselves, such as accidental qualities like colors and tastes, and involuntary movements like tremors. This shows the Qadariyya boundary of human power.

Key Terms
الاضطرار
الاختيارية
الاستقلال
مُخترع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qadariyya completely deny any divine creation in human actions.
Correction: The passage shows Qadariyya accept that Allah creates involuntary attributes like colors and tastes, and involuntary movements like tremors.

Checkpoint
According to the Qadariyya as described, which of the following is NOT independently created by the animal?
A) voluntary movement
B) color of skin
C) walking
D) sitting

Key Takeaway
The Jabriyya and Qadariyya represent two errors: one denies human power entirely, the other exaggerates it by claiming independent creation of voluntary acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a good decision, do you feel it is entirely your accomplishment? This lesson shows two extremes: one denies your role entirely, the other overestimates it. Which inclination do you struggle with more—and how does that affect your gratitude toward Allah and your humility?
?
The Jabriyya say you have no power; the Qadariyya say you need no help. Both are wrong. In your daily life, when you feel helpless, do you lean toward despair (like Jabriyya) or self-reliance (like Qadariyya)? How can you find the balanced path of acknowledging Allah’s power while still striving with the ability He gives you?
?
Sanusi highlights that even the Qadariyya admit Allah creates involuntary acts like colors and smells. Where in your life do you attribute good outcomes solely to your own efforts, forgetting that Allah created even the ability you used? What specific situation will you reflect on tonight to remind yourself of His role in your actions?

Qadariyya's Theory of Direct and Generated Actions
Sanusi details the Qadariyya division of actions into direct and generated, then asserts Sunni avoidance of both extremes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qadariyya sect classified human actions into direct and generated categories, and why Ahl al-Sunna ultimately rejects this framework.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya divided human power into two layers — one inside the limb, one outside.

The Qadariyya developed a detailed theory of how human agents create their own actions. They distinguished between actions that occur directly in the limb where power resides, and those that occur outside that locus, which they called 'generated' actions. This passage presents their classification with examples, and then begins to contrast it with the position of Ahl al-Sunna.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Qadariyya's first category — direct actions
The passage explains that certain actions like moving the hand or sitting occur 'in the locus of power' and are directly created by the agent. Identify these examples.
Direct actions are within the body where power is located.
2
Understand the second category — generated actions
Actions like moving a stone or shooting an arrow occur outside the locus of power. The agent creates them indirectly by first creating a direct movement. This is 'generation' (tawallud).
Note the example of moving a stone via hand movement.
3
Reflect on the effect of physical strength
The passage notes that the effect of generation varies with strength of nerves and limbs. This shows the Qadariyya attempted to account for physical causality.
This links theology with observable reality.
4
Consider Ahl al-Sunna's overall stance
Sanusi states that the Sunni position avoids both Qadariyya and Jabriyya extremes, combining truth and Shari'a. Even though the passage is cut short, we see the rejection of the Qadariyya framework.
This sets up the next lesson(s) on the Sunni view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قالوا: إنَّ الحيوان في اختراعِهِ لأفعاله الاختيارية على ضربين: ما وُجِدَ منها في محلّ قُوَّتِهِ: كحركاته وسكناته، وقيامه وقعودِهِ ومشيه وجريه؛ فهو مُخترع له مباشرةً. وما وُجِدَ منها خارجاً عن محلّ قُوَّتِهِ: كتحريك الحجر والسهم، والضرب بالسيف والرمح، والقتلِ والجَرْحِ ونحو ذلكَ؛ فهو مُخترِعُهُ تولُّداً؛ أي: بواسطة اختراعه لحركاتٍ في محلّ قُوَّتِهِ. ويختلفُ أثرُ التولدِ عندَهم باختلافِ قُوَّةِ العَصَبِ والأعضاءِ وضعفها، ولهذا كانَتْ حقيقة التولُّدِ عندهم: وجود حادثٍ عن مقدور بالقدرة الحادثة، فحركةُ الحَجَرِ مثلاً مُتولّد عندهم؛ لأنَّهُ حادث نشأ عن شيء مقدور بالقدرة الحادثة؛ وهو حركة اليد والاعتماد بها مثلاً. ومذهب أهل السنَّةِ والحقِّ مُجانِب لكلا المذهبينِ الفاسدين، وقد جمع بفضل الله بين الحقيقةِ والشريعةِ، وسَلِمَ بتوفيقِ اللهِ تعالى مِنْ
Then they said: The animal, in its invention of its voluntary actions, is of two types: what exists of them in the locus of its power, like its movements and rests, its standing and sitting, its walking and running; so it is its invention directly. And what exists of them outside the locus of its power, like moving a stone and an arrow, hitting with a sword and a spear, killing and wounding and the like; so it is its invention by generation, i.e., by means of its invention of movements in the locus of its power. And the effect of generation differs according to the strength and weakness of nerves and limbs. For this reason, the reality of generation according to them is: the existence of an event from something that is within power by the temporal power; thus the movement of a stone, for example, is generated according to them, because it is an event that originated from something that is within power by the temporal power, namely the movement of the hand and relying on it, for example. And the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna and the truth avoids both corrupt doctrines, and by Allah's grace it combines truth and Shari'a, and by Allah's guidance it was saved from...
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage explains the Qadariyya's detailed theory of how human power creates actions. They distinguish two types: (1) Direct actions: those that occur within the limb where the power resides, like movements, resting, sitting, walking – these the agent creates directly. (2) Generated actions: those that occur outside the limb, like moving a stone, shooting an arrow, hitting with a sword – these the agent creates indirectly by first creating a movement in the limb which then causes the external effect. Generation differs based on physical strength. The author gives the example of moving a stone: the hand movement is directly created, and the stone's movement is generated. Then the author concludes that Ahl al-Sunna avoids both extremes (Jabriyya and Qadariyya) and combines truth and law, being saved from error.

Key Terms
محل القوة
مباشرة
تولد
مقدور
القوة الحادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qadariyya concept of generated actions is the same as natural causality or secondary causation.
Correction: The Qadariyya's generated actions are theological: the agent creates the external effect indirectly by first creating the direct action. It is not a natural law but a form of human power extending beyond the limb.

Checkpoint
According to the Qadariyya, which action is considered 'generated'?
A) walking
B) moving a stone
C) moving the hand
D) sitting

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya divided voluntary actions into those directly created within the power's locus and those generated outside it, but Ahl al-Sunna rejects this as an extreme view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action that affects something outside you—like typing a key or throwing a ball—do you ever pause to consider that your power to do so is not truly your own? This lesson shows how the Qadariyya attempted to define human power precisely. In what area of your life do you most feel a false sense of independence from Allah? This week, before each action that causes an external effect, silently say 'There is no power except by Allah' and see how it changes your awareness.
?
The Qadariyya divided actions into two categories, but the Sunni avoids both overconfidence and fatalism. Think of a time you succeeded in something difficult. Did you silently credit yourself, or did you immediately thank Allah? How can you train your heart to attribute every direct and generated effect to the Creator? Write down one specific habit you will adopt to redirect praise to Allah.
?
This passage says Ahl al-Sunna 'combines truth and Shari'a'. Reflect: Is there an area of your life where you act as if you are the sole cause (like the Qadariyya) or as if you have no responsibility (like the Jabriyya)? Describe a concrete situation and commit to a specific action that realigns your belief with the middle path of Islam.

Exonerating the Imams from Problematic Statements on Divine Power
Sanusi refutes opinions on divine power and defends imams via ta'wil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's method of exonerating Sunni imams from problematic statements about divine power by interpreting them as dialectical tools used in debates with heretics.

Core Idea
The imams' seemingly heretical statements were dialectical tactics, not their true beliefs.

When studying Islamic theology, you may come across statements from great scholars that seem to contradict core Sunni beliefs. In this passage, Sanusi addresses precisely this issue regarding divine power. He not only refutes the problematic statements but also defends the integrity of the imams who reportedly made them, explaining that they were engaged in dialectical argumentation with heretics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three transmitted opinions
Read the passage and list the three opinions attributed to al-Taftazani, Abu Ishaq, and Imam al-Haramayn. Notice how each opinion seems to limit divine power in some way.
The opinions are about whether originated power influences an act or a mere aspect.
2
Understand Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi declares these opinions corrupt and contradictory to reason and revelation. He refers to his other works for full refutation. Recognize that he does not discuss the details here but asserts their falsehood.
He relies on the reader's acceptance that God's power encompasses all possibles.
3
Grasp the exoneration principle
Sanusi argues that the imams' actual books affirm the opposite: God's power and will are universal. He cites consensus of the Salaf and al-Qadi's transmission of consensus on unbelief of those who deny this. This shows the imams did not hold those views.
This principle protects the imams' orthodoxy despite the reports.
4
Learn the ta'wil explanation
The statements, if authentic, were said only as dialectical arguments. The imams temporarily adopted the heretics' premises to expose their corruption. Understand that this is a common method in kalam debates.
This final step resolves the apparent contradiction and preserves Sunni doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التفتازانيُّ الأخصَّ في ( شرح المقاصدِ الدينية » له(۱) ونُقِلَ عنِ الأستاذ أبي إسحاق : مثله ، إلا أنَّهُ لمَّا كانَ يقولُ بنفي الأحوالِ عبّر عن أخص وصْفِ الفعل بالوجه والاعتبارِ ؛ فقال : القدرة الحادثةُ تُؤثر في وجه و اعتبار (۲) ومنها : ما نُقِلَ عن إمام الحرمين في آخرِ أمرِهِ : أَنَّ القدرة الحادثة تُؤثر في وجودِ الفعل على وَفْقِ مشيئة المولى تبارك وتعالى (٣) . ولا يخفى فساد هذه الأقوال ومصادمتها للعقل والشرع ، وقد أشبعنا الكلامَ في ردّها في شرحِنا على عقيدتنا الكبرى » ، و« شرحنا على عقيدتنا الوسطى » (٤) والواجب تنزيه هؤلاء الأئمَّةِ عن اعتقادِ ظاهرِ ما نُقِلَ عنهم ؛ لأنَّ الموجود في كتبهم الكلاميَّةِ إِنَّما هو ضد هذا المنقول عنهم ؛ وهو تعميم قدرة الله تعالى وإرادتِهِ لجميعِ الممكناتِ ، ونقلوا إجماع السلف الصالح على ذلك وقد نقل القاضي رحمهُ اللهُ تعالى الإجماع في مواضعَ مِنْ كتبِهِ على كُفْرِ مَنْ نسب الاختراع لغير الله تعالى ، ونقل أيضاً إجماع الأمة على كفرِ مَنْ لم يقل بعموم صفاتِ الباري تبارك وتعالى (١) . ويجب تأويلُ ما صدر عنهم إن صح النقلُ بِهِ أَنَّهُ إِنَّما قالوه على سبيلِ الجدل في مناظرة الخصومِ مِنَ المبتدعة ، وإلزامهم على مقتضى أصولهم الفاسدة أقوالاً فاسدة لم يقولوا بها ؛ ليُظْهِروا لهم أنَّهم لم يبنوا فيما يقولونَ على أساس صحيح ، وإنَّما يبنون أقوالهم على أساس فاسد ، فمهما بَنَوا عليه قولاً رمته رياح الجدل ، وألزمتهم أنْ يُجدِّدوا على ذلك الأساس الفاسدِ بناءً آخرَ فاسداً لا ثبات له ، ويوافقون على عدم استقرارِهِ ، للكنْ أُلزموا أنْ يبنوه لاقتضاء أساسِهمُ الفاسدِ إِيَّاهُ وهذا ظاهر (۲) ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق .
Al-Taftazani [said] the most specific attribute in (Sharh al-Maqasid al-Diniyya) (1). And it was transmitted from al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq: similar to it, except that because he used to deny states, he expressed the most specific description of the act by 'aspect and consideration'; so he said: the originated power influences in an aspect and consideration (2). And among them: what was transmitted from Imam al-Haramayn at the end of his affair: that the originated power influences the existence of the act in accordance with the will of the Master, Blessed and Exalted (3). And the corruption of these statements and their contradiction of reason and revelation is not hidden, and we have given full speech in refuting them in our commentary on our 'Aqida al-Kubra' and our commentary on our 'Aqida al-Wusta' (4). And it is obligatory to exonerate these imams from believing the apparent meaning of what has been transmitted from them, because what is found in their theological books is the opposite of what has been transmitted from them; and it is the generalization of the power of Allah Most High and His will for all possibles, and they transmitted the consensus of the righteous predecessors on that. And al-Qadi (may Allah have mercy on him) transmitted consensus in places of his books on the unbelief of one who ascribes creation to other than Allah Most High, and he also transmitted the consensus of the Ummah on the unbelief of one who does not affirm the generality of the attributes of the Creator, Blessed and Exalted (1). And it is necessary to interpret what issued from them, if the transmission is authentic, that they only said it by way of dialectical argument in debating opponents from the innovators, and imposing upon them, according to their corrupt principles, corrupt statements which they themselves did not hold, in order to show them that they (the imams) do not build their statements on a sound foundation, but rather they build their statements on a corrupt foundation. So whenever they built a statement upon it, the winds of debate would blow it away, and they would be compelled to build upon that corrupt foundation another corrupt building with no stability, and they would agree on its instability, but they were compelled to build it due to the requirement of their corrupt foundation. And this is apparent (2), and with Allah Most High is success.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of certain transmitted statements about divine power and human action, combined with a defense of the imams from whom these statements are reported. The author first presents three transmitted opinions: al-Taftazani's specification of an 'aspect' of power, Abu Ishaq's similar view with the condition of denying states, and Imam al-Haramayn's later view that the originated power influences the act in accordance with God's will. The author then declares these statements to be corrupt and contradictory to both reason and revelation, referring to his own detailed refutations elsewhere. He argues that the imams must be exonerated from believing the apparent meaning of these reports, because their actual theological works affirm the opposite: that God's power and will encompass all possibles. He cites the consensus of the righteous predecessors and al-Qadi's transmission of consensus on the unbelief of those who ascribe creation to others or deny the universality of God's attributes. Finally, he explains the necessity of interpreting (ta'wil) these statements, if authentically reported, as dialectical devices used in debates with heretics. The imams would temporarily adopt the heretics' faulty premises to show that even on those premises, the heretics' own arguments collapse, forcing them into further absurdities. This demonstrates that the imams did not actually hold those views but used them for refutation.

Key Terms
القدرة الحادثة
الأحوال
الجدل
التأويل
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the quoted statements (al-Taftazani, Abu Ishaq, Imam al-Haramayn) represent the actual doctrine of these imams.
Correction: Sanusi argues that these statements must be interpreted as dialectical devices; the imams' own books and the consensus of the Salaf affirm the opposite.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi insist on interpreting (ta'wil) the statements of al-Taftazani, Abu Ishaq, and Imam al-Haramayn?
A) Because the narrators were weak and untrustworthy
B) Because their own books affirm the opposite, and the statements were dialectical arguments
C) Because they were forced to say them under duress by heretics
D) Because they later publicly recanted these views

Key Takeaway
Sanusi teaches that apparent problematic statements from Sunni imams must be interpreted as dialectical arguments, not as their actual beliefs, thereby preserving the integrity of both the imams and Sunni doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a statement from a respected scholar that seems to contradict what you believe, do you immediately reject the scholar or do you pause to consider the context—perhaps it was said in a heated debate or with a specific audience in mind? What does this lesson teach you about assuming good of the scholars, even when their words appear problematic?
?
Sanusi argues that the imams engaged in jadal (dialectical argument) to expose the faultiness of heretical premises. Reflect on your own conversations about religion: are you more interested in winning an argument or in uncovering the truth? How often do you adopt someone else's flawed premise just to show them it leads to absurdity? Is that a temptation for your own ego?
?
This lesson warns against taking statements out of context. Have you ever misunderstood someone's words because you ignored their intention or the setting? How can you apply the principle of ta'wil (interpretation) in your daily interactions—not only with scholars but with family and friends—to build bridges instead of walls?

Six Types of Shirk According to Sanusi
Sanusi enumerates six types of shirk with definitions and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six types of shirk as defined by Sanusi, their distinguishing features, and the key footnote clarifying that shirk does not always entail kufr.

Core Idea
Shirk comes in six forms, from dualism to acting for show.

Sanusi lays out a taxonomy of shirk, moving from gross polytheism to subtle acts of worship for other than Allah. Each type has a distinct logic and historical example. The footnotes refine the definition, noting that not all shirk is outright disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes footnotes marked (1) and (2).
2
Identify the six types
The passage presents six types of shirk. Find each one and note its name in Arabic and English.
3
Understand each definition
For each type, read the definition Sanusi gives. Ask yourself: what is the core idea? How does it differ from the others?
Pay attention to the key terms: استقلال, تبغيض, تقريب, تقليد, أسباب, أغراض.
4
Note the examples
Sanusi provides a historical or sectarian example for each type. Identify which group or period is mentioned and why.
The examples help contextualise the abstract definitions.
5
Analyse the footnotes
The two footnotes add important clarifications. The first defines shirk broadly. The second explains why 'independent' is specified for the first type.
This distinguishes major from minor shirk and refines the concept of dualism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة
في أنواع الشِّرْكِ
وَأَنْوَاعُ الشِّرْكِ سِتَّةُ (١):
- شِرْكُ اسْتِقْلالٍ: وَهُوَ إِثْبَاتُ إِلَهَيْنِ مُسْتَقِلَيْنِ (۲) كَشِرْكِ الْمَجُوسِ.
- وَشِرْكُ تَبْغِيض: وَهُوَ تَرْكِيبُ الإِلَهِ مِنْ آلِهَةٍ؛ كَشِرْكِ النَّصَارَى.
- وَشِرْكُ تَقْرِيبٍ: وَهُوَ عِبَادَةُ غَيْرِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى لِيُقَرِّبَ إِلَى اللهِ تَعَالَى زُلْفَى؛ كَشِرْكِ مُتَقَدِّمِي الْجَاهِلِيَّةِ.
- وَشِرْكُ تَقْلِيدٍ: وَهُوَ عِبَادَةُ غَيْرِ اللهِ تَعَالَى تَبَعاً لِلْغَيْرِ؛ كَشِرْكِ مُتَأَخِّرِي الْجَاهِلِيَّةِ.
- وَشِرْكُ أَسْبَابٍ: وَهُوَ إِسْنَادُ التَّأْثِيرِ لِلأَسْبَابِ الْعَادِيَّةِ؛ كَشِرْكِ الْفَلَاسِفَةِ وَالطَّبَائِعِيِّينَ وَمَنْ تَبِعَهُمْ عَلَى ذَلِكَ.
- وَشِرْكُ الأَغْرَاضِ: وَهُوَ الْعَمَلُ لِغَيْرِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى.
(١) المراد بالشرك هنا: مجرَّدُ إدخال الغير مع الله تعالى، لزم منه كفر أو لم يلزم.
(۲) التقييد بـ (مستقلين) مع وجود الاثنينية؛ للتنصيص على عدم الاشتراك في الأثر كما سيظهر؛ إذ معنى الانفكاك بينهما مفاد من التثنية، ولذا يقال لهم: الثنوية.
The Third Introduction: On the Types of Shirk. The types of shirk are six: (1) Shirk of Independence: it is affirming two independent gods, like the shirk of the Magians. (2) Shirk of Composition: it is composing the god from multiple gods, like the shirk of the Christians. (3) Shirk of Approximation: it is worshipping other than Allah the Exalted in order to draw near to Allah the Exalted in closeness, like the shirk of the early Jahiliyyah. (4) Shirk of Imitation: it is worshipping other than Allah the Exalted following others, like the shirk of the later Jahiliyyah. (5) Shirk of Causes: it is attributing effect to natural causes, like the shirk of the philosophers and the naturalists and those who follow them in that. (6) Shirk of Purposes: it is acting for other than Allah the Exalted. (1) What is meant by shirk here: merely bringing in another with Allah the Exalted, whether disbelief necessarily follows from it or not. (2) The restriction to (two independent ones) despite the existence of duality; to specify the absence of sharing in effect as will appear; for the meaning of separation between them is understood from the duality, and thus they are called: the Dualists.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a classification. Sanusi presents six types of shirk, each with a definition and an example. He begins with the title 'The Third Introduction: On the Types of Shirk.' He states that there are six types. The first is Shirk al-Istiqlal (independence), which is affirming two separate, independent gods, as did the Magians (Zoroastrians). The second is Shirk al-Tabghid (composition), which is constructing the god from multiple deities, as Christians do with the Trinity. The third is Shirk al-Taqrib (approximation), worshipping other than Allah to draw near to Him, as the early pagans of the Jahiliyyah did. The fourth is Shirk al-Taqlid (imitation), worshipping other than Allah merely out of following others, as the later Jahiliyyah did. The fifth is Shirk al-Asbab (causes), attributing causal effect to natural means, as the philosophers and naturalists do. The sixth is Shirk al-Aghrad (purposes), performing actions for other than Allah. Two footnotes follow. The first clarifies that shirk here means any association of another with Allah, whether or not it constitutes outright disbelief (kufr). This distinguishes major and minor shirk. The second footnote explains why the definition of Shirk al-Istiqlal specifies 'two independent [gods]'—to emphasize that they do not share in effect (i.e., they are separate in action), which is implied by the duality, and thus their adherents are called Dualists.

أغراض
مجوس
نصارى
جاهلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shirk always entails kufr (disbelief).
Correction: Sanusi's footnote explains that shirk here means merely associating another with Allah, whether or not it leads to kufr. This allows for lesser shirk that does not remove one from the fold of Islam.

Checkpoint
Which type of shirk is exemplified by the Christians according to Sanusi?

B) Shirk al-Tabghid
C) Shirk al-Asbab
D) Shirk al-Aghrad

Key Takeaway
Sanusi classifies shirk into six types, each with a distinct definition and historical example, and clarifies that shirk does not always constitute disbelief.

?

?
Sanusi mentions Shirk al-Aghrad (acting for other than Allah). Think of the last good deed you did. Was your intention purely for Allah, or was there a hint of showing off or seeking praise? What concrete step will you take to purify your intention in your next act of worship?
?
The lesson distinguishes between shirk that leads to disbelief and shirk that does not. Do you take minor shirk lightly? What is one form of minor shirk (like riya') that you struggle with, and how will you actively fight it this week?

The Magian Argument for Dualism
Sanusi explains the Magians' dualism and its two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualistic belief of the Magians and the two arguments they used to justify their creed.

Core Idea
Opposing motives require separate gods — the Magian argument for dualism.

Sanusi introduces the Magians, a pre-Islamic dualist sect, and explains the reasoning behind their belief in two independent deities: one for good and one for evil. Understanding their argument helps clarify why Islamic theology emphasizes the oneness of God and the compatibility of all attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: first the Magians' claim, then two supporting arguments.
The passage is from a refutation, so it presents their view before critiquing it.
2
Identify the two arguments
The Magians give two reasons for believing in two separate gods. Find each one and note how they differ.
The first argument is based on opposing motives; the second on incompatible descriptions.
3
Consider why this is considered shirk
Sanusi frames their belief as shirk. Think about why attributing independent power to two beings violates tawhid.
Islamic theology holds that Allah alone has absolute power over good and evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا المجوس : فالحامل لهم على الشرك الذي انتحلوه : اعتقادهم أنَّ فعلَ الخير يجبُ أنْ يكونَ لهُ باعث يباينُ الباعث على فعل الشر ، وإذا تباينا لم يمكن أن يجتمعا في ذاتِ واحدة(۱) ، فوجب التعدُّد في ذات الإله ، فلزمَ إثباتُ إلهينِ مُستقلين (۲) ؛ أحدهما يستقل بفعل الخير ، ويُسمَّى عندهم : ( هرمز ) ، والآخر يستقل بفعل الشر ، ويُسمَّى عندهم : ( أزدانَ ) (۳)
وأيضاً : ففاعلُ الخيرِ يُسمَّى خَيْراً ، وفاعلُ الشرِّ يُسمَّى شريراً (٤) والوصفانِ متباينان لا يمكنُ اجتماعُهُما في موصوفٍ واحدٍ ، فوجبَ أَنْ يكون موصوفهما اثنين .
As for the Magians: what impelled them to the shirk they adopted is their belief that the act of good must have a motive that opposes the motive for the act of evil, and when they oppose each other it is impossible for them to come together in a single essence, so multiplicity in the essence of divinity became necessary, thus the affirmation of two independent gods became necessary; one of them independently performs good, and is called by them Hormazd, and the other independently performs evil, and is called by them Azdana. And also: the doer of good is called good, and the doer of evil is called evil, and the two descriptions are mutually exclusive, it is impossible for both to come together in a single described object, so their described object must be two.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the dualistic creed of the Magians (Zoroastrians). The author gives two arguments that the Magians use to justify their belief in two separate gods: first, they argue that the motives for good and evil are opposite and cannot reside in one being, so there must be two distinct deities—one for good (Hormazd) and one for evil (Azdana). Second, they argue that the agent of good is called 'good' and the agent of evil is called 'evil', and these two descriptions are incompatible in a single subject, so two subjects are required. The author does not explicitly refute these arguments in this passage, but he frames them as the 'shirk' that the Magians adopted, implying their invalidity. The footnote markers indicate further commentary in the margins, but the main text here simply presents the Magian reasoning.

ثنوية
إلهين مستقلين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that dualism is a logical necessity because good and evil seem opposite.
Correction: Islamic theology holds that Allah is the creator of both good and evil, and His attributes are not limited by human understanding of opposition.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Magians argued that opposing actions require separate divine agents, but Islamic theology affirms that Allah alone has power over all things, including good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would change in your prayers if you truly believed that Allah alone is behind everything that happens to you?
?
The Magians could not accept that one God could will both good and evil, so they split the world into two creators. Are there areas of your life where you try to compartmentalize – giving credit to Allah for the good but blaming yourself, others, or fate for the bad? How will you train yourself to bring your whole life, including your struggles, under the oneness of Allah?

The Magian Doctrine of Dualism
Sanusi presents the Magian doctrine and their two arguments for dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Magian dualist doctrine and the two rational arguments they used to justify belief in two gods.

Core Idea
The Magians argued that good and evil require separate divine sources.

Sanusi presents the Magian position before refuting it. Understanding their arguments is essential for grasping why Islamic theology insists on divine unity. This lesson examines the two arguments they advanced.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: first the general claim, then two specific arguments.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage presents two distinct arguments. Find the first argument about motives for good and evil, and the second about incompatible descriptions.
The first argument uses 'باعث' (motive), the second uses 'الوصفان المتباينان' (incompatible descriptions).
3
Note the terminology
The Magians call the god of good Hormuz and the god of evil Azdan. Understand that these are proper names.
These names are not found in Islamic sources; they are from Magian tradition.
4
Reflect on plausibility
Think about why someone might find these arguments convincing. This prepares you for Sanusi's refutation.
Consider the logical structure: differences require distinct sources.
5
Prepare for refutation
After understanding the Magian position, consider how an Islamic theologian might respond. Sanusi will refute these arguments in the subsequent passage.
Key point: the unity of God is not contradicted by the existence of evil – Islamic theology explains this differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عقيدة المجوس ، وبيان فسادها ] أمَّا المجوس : فالحامل لهم على الشرك الذي انتحلوه : اعتقادهم أنَّ فعلَ الخير يجبُ أنْ يكونَ لهُ باعث يباينُ الباعث على فعل الشر ، وإذا تباينا لم يمكن أن يجتمعا في ذاتِ واحدة، فوجب التعدُّد في ذات الإله ، فلزمَ إثباتُ إلهينِ مُستقلين؛ أحدهما يستقل بفعل الخير ، ويُسمَّى عندهم : ( هرمز ) ، والآخر يستقل بفعل الشر ، ويُسمَّى عندهم : ( أزدانَ ) وأيضاً : ففاعلُ الخيرِ يُسمَّى خَيْراً ، وفاعلُ الشرِّ يُسمَّى شريراً والوصفانِ متباينان لا يمكنُ اجتماعُهُما في موصوفٍ واحدٍ ، فوجبَ أَنْ يكون موصوفهما اثنين .
The doctrine of the Magians, and an exposition of its corruption. As for the Magians: what drives them to the polytheism they have adopted is their belief that the act of good must have a motive that is different from the motive for the act of evil, and when they differ, they cannot combine in a single essence, so multiplicity in the essence of the divine is necessary, and thus the affirmation of two independent gods becomes necessary; one of them is independent in doing good, and is called by them 'Hormuz', and the other is independent in doing evil, and is called by them 'Azdan'. Also: the doer of good is called good, and the doer of evil is called evil, and these two descriptions are incompatible, so they cannot be combined in a single described entity; thus it is necessary that their described subject be two.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents the Magian (Zoroastrian) doctrine of dualism, including two arguments they use to justify belief in two gods. First argument: Since good and evil acts require different motives, and these motives cannot coexist in one essence, there must be two separate divine essences—one for good (Hormuz) and one for evil (Azdan). Second argument: The doer of good is called 'good' and the doer of evil is called 'evil'; these descriptions are mutually exclusive, so they must belong to two distinct subjects. Sanusi is setting up the Magian position before refuting it.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Magians only argued from the problem of evil (why does evil exist if God is good?), but they also argued from the incompatibility of descriptions.
Correction: Sanusi shows two distinct arguments: one based on different motives for good and evil acts, and another based on the logical incompatibility of the descriptions 'good' and 'evil' in one subject.

Observation Refutes Dualism
Sanusi refutes dualism by noting that in the observable world, one agent does both good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses empirical observation to refute the dualist claim that good and evil must have separate creators.

Core Idea
Observation proves that a single agent does both good and evil, undermining dualism.

Sanusi presents a second argument against the Magian dualists. He points out that if good and evil must come from separate sources, then no single creature could perform both. But everyday observation shows that humans do exactly that. This contradiction refutes the dualist principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the argument without interpretation.
Notice the key terms 'الشاهد' (the witnessed realm) and 'المشاهدة' (observation).
2
Understand the dualist premise
The Magians claim that good and evil require separate gods. This implies that any agent of good cannot be an agent of evil, and vice versa.
3
See the contradiction from observation
Sanusi argues that in the created world, we observe creatures (like humans) who perform both good and evil acts. This directly contradicts the dualist premise.
Observation (المشاهدة) renders the premise false.
4
Conclude the refutation
Since the premise leads to a false conclusion, the premise itself is invalid. Dualism is thus refuted by empirical evidence.
This is a classic argument from contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فيلزمهم في الشاهدِ أَنَّ الفاعل مِنَ المخلوقات للخير لا يمكنُ أنْ يكون فاعلاً للشرِّ ، وفاعل الشر لا يمكن أن يكون فاعلاً للخير ، والمشاهدة تقتضي بطلان ذلك .
And also, it follows for them in the witnessed [realm] that the doer among creatures of good cannot be a doer of evil, and the doer of evil cannot be a doer of good, but observation necessitates the invalidity of that.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
In this second refutation, Sanusi appeals to empirical observation (al-shahid). The Magians' principle would imply that in the created world, one agent cannot perform both good and evil. Yet we observe that humans, for example, do both good and evil acts. This contradiction shows that the Magian premise is false. Therefore, the argument that good and evil require separate gods is not supported by experience.

Key Terms
الشاهد
المخلوقات
المشاهدة
بطلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) By noting that humans do both good and evil.
B) By noting that the sun gives both light and darkness.
C) By noting that the gods are invisible.
D) By noting that prayer is ineffective.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes dualism by showing that observation contradicts the claim that good and evil require separate agents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see yourself doing both good and evil every day. Do you ever subconsciously act as if these come from different parts of you—excusing your evil as 'not the real me'? What would it mean to truly own both your good and evil actions before God? Will you today take full responsibility for a sin you've been attributing to circumstance?
?
Sanusi uses simple observation to destroy a false belief. What false belief about yourself—perhaps that you're too weak to change or too advanced to fall—is contradicted by what you actually observe in your life? What will you do today to bring that belief in line with truth?

Refutation of the Christian Trinity
Sanusi describes the Christian Trinity and points out its internal contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's rational refutation of the Christian Trinity based on the contradiction between unity and multiplicity.

Core Idea
The Trinity combines two contradictories: unity and multiplicity.

In this passage, Sanusi critiques the Christian doctrine of the Trinity on logical grounds. He argues that Christians, observing effects requiring multiple causes in the world, mistakenly applied this to God, leading to a doctrine that asserts both unity and multiplicity — a clear contradiction. Let's examine his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Christian rationale
Sanusi notes that Christians derived the Trinity from observing that effects in the visible world often require multiple causes. He then presents their claim that God is composed of three hypostases.
2
Identify the contradiction
Sanusi points out that Christians call these three hypostases 'gods' while also saying the three are one god. He calls this a combination of two contradictories: unity and multiplicity.
This is the core of the refutation.
3
Analyze the incoherence
Sanusi further criticizes that they reduce the divine essence to abstract concepts (states or aspects) that have no external reality, which is rationally unintelligible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النصارى أهلكَهمُ الله تعالى : فإنَّهم لما رأَوْا توقف الفعل في الشاهد - كنبات الزرع ووجودِ الثمار ونحوهما ـ على تعدُّدِ المؤثر . . قالوا تعالى الله عن قولهم : الإلهُ مُركَّبٌ مِنْ ثلاثةِ أقانيم : وهي أُقنوم الوجودِ ، وأقنوم العلم ، وأقنوم الحياة ، وحكموا عليها بأنها آلهة ثلاثةٌ معَ أَنَّها صفات ! ثم قالوا مع ذلكَ : إنَّما مجموع الثلاثة إله واحدٌ ! فجمعوا بينَ نقيضين : وحدة وكثرة ، وجعلوا الذات تتركَّبُ مِنْ مُجرَّدِ أحوال لا وجود لها ، أو وجوه واعتباراتٍ لا توجد إلا في الأذهان ، وذلكَ غير معقول لعاقل .
As for the Christians – may Allah destroy them – when they saw that action in the visible world – like the growth of plants, the existence of fruits, and similar things – depends on a multiplicity of effectors, they said – exalted is Allah above their saying – that the god is composed of three hypostases (aqānīm): the hypostasis of existence, the hypostasis of knowledge, and the hypostasis of life, and they judged them to be three gods while they are attributes! Then they said, despite that: the sum of the three is one god! So they combined two contradictories: unity and multiplicity, and they made the essence composed of mere states (aḥwāl) that have no existence, or aspects and considerations that exist only in minds, and that is unintelligible to any rational person.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage refutes the Christian doctrine of the Trinity. Sanusi begins by stating that Christians observed that effects in the phenomenal world often require multiple causes, so they inferred that the divine must be composite. They then posited that God is composed of three hypostases (aqānīm): existence, knowledge, and life. Sanusi points out that these are actually attributes, not distinct persons. Despite calling them three gods, they also claim the three are one god. This is a contradiction: they simultaneously assert unity and multiplicity. Furthermore, they reduce the divine essence to abstract concepts that have no external reality. Sanusi concludes that this notion is rationally unintelligible.

Key Terms
أقنوم (hypostasis)
صفات (attributes)
نقيضين (two contradictories)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Christian Trinity is a logical mystery beyond human reason.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the Trinity is not a mystery but a clear logical contradiction (unity and multiplicity) and thus rationally impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what contradiction do Christians commit in their doctrine of the Trinity?
A) They claim God is both one and three.
B) They claim God has no attributes.
C) They deny the existence of God.
D) They affirm that God is composed of matter.

Key Takeaway
The Christian Trinity is refuted on logical grounds because it combines unity and multiplicity, which are contradictories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a religious belief that appears logically contradictory, do you accept it as a 'mystery' or do you investigate it? How does your response affect the strength of your faith in Allah's perfect oneness? Commit now to never compromise on the principle of tawheed, even when faced with seemingly sophisticated arguments.
?
Sanusi uses sharp language ('may Allah destroy them') in refuting the Christians. Do you ever find yourself feeling a similar disdain for those who hold incorrect beliefs? How can you maintain firmness in truth while still showing mercy and wisdom in da'wah? Ask Allah to purify your intentions.
?
Reflect on the fact that the Christians took observed natural effects (needing multiple causes) and applied it to God. Are there any areas where you have incorrectly applied human reasoning to Allah's nature? Pray for protection from falling into anthropomorphism or false analogies.

Refutation of Three Christian Incarnation Interpretations
Sanusi refutes the three interpretations of the union of the Word with Jesus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Christian doctrine of incarnation fails on all three proposed interpretations according to Sanusi.

Core Idea
The three rational explanations of incarnation all collapse under logical scrutiny.

Sanusi presents a powerful refutation of the Christian doctrine of the union of the Divine Word with Jesus. He first states the general claim, then examines three different interpretations that Christians have offered—subsistence, mixture, and impression—and shows each to be rationally impossible. This passage demonstrates the application of rational argument to theological claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the three interpretations
The passage presents three interpretations of the union (ittiḥād): subsistence as accident, mixture, and impression. Find each one and notice how Sanusi defines it before refuting it.
Look for key terms: قيام (subsistence), اختلاط (mixture), انطباع (impression).
3
Analyse each refutation
For each interpretation, note the logical problem Sanusi raises. He uses self-evident principles: one meaning cannot subsist in two essences; mixture is physical but the Word is not; impression only produces a likeness.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Sanusi concludes that the Christian doctrine is unintelligible and that Christians are the most deficient in understanding. Consider why he makes such a strong judgment based on rational arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم زعموا أيضاً : أنَّ أقنوم العلم منها ـ ويُسمَّى : الكلمة ـ اتَّحد بناسوت عيسى ؛ أي : جسدِهِ ، فكان إلها بسبب ذلكَ واختلفوا في معنى اتحادِ الكلمة به : فمنهم مَنْ فَسَّرَهُ بقيام الكلمةِ بهِ كما يقوم العرضُ بالجوهر : وهذا يُوجِبُ مفارقته لذات الجوهر الذي هو عندهم مجموع الأقانيم الثلاثة ، وهم يقولون : اتَّحد اللاهوتُ بناسوت عيسى عليهِ السلامُ مِنْ غيرِ أنْ يُفارِقَ ذات الجوهرِ ، ومِنَ المعلوم ضرورة : أنَّ المعنى الواحد لا يقوم بذاتين ومنهم مَنْ فَسَّرَ هذا الاتحاد بالاختلاط والمزج : كاختلاط الخمرِ مع الماء ونحوهما مِنَ المائعاتِ ، وكيف يُعقَلُ الاختلاط الحسي الذي هو مِنْ صفاتِ الأجسام في الكلمة التي هي معنى مِنَ المعاني ، بل هي حال عندهم وخاصيَّة للذات الأزليَّة ؟! ومنهم مَنْ فَسَّرَ الاتحاد بالانطباع : كانطباع صورة النقش في الشمع ، ومعلوم أنَّ نقش ذلك الشيء لم يحصل فيما طُبِعَ فيهِ ، وإِنَّما يحصل فيه مثاله . فانظر إلى هذا المذهب الركيكِ ما أخسَّهُ وأرذلَهُ ! وهو مذهب غيرُ معقول ، والنصارى أخسُّ الفِرَقِ كلّها وأرذلها أفهاماً ، وإدراك الحقائقِ على مثلهم عسير
Then they also claimed that the hypostasis of knowledge – called the Word – united with the human nature of Jesus, i.e., his body, so he became a god because of that. And they differed over the meaning of the Word's union with him. Some interpreted it as the Word subsisting in him as an accident subsists in a substance: this necessitates its separation from the essence of the substance which, according to them, is the sum of the three hypostases, while they say: the divine nature united with the human nature of Jesus, peace be upon him, without separating from the essence of the substance, and it is self-evident that one meaning cannot subsist in two essences. Some interpreted this union as mixture and blending: like the mixing of wine with water and similar liquids. How can physical mixture – which is a property of bodies – be conceived in the Word which is a meaning among meanings? Rather, it is, according to them, a state and a property of the eternal essence? And some interpreted the union as impression: like the impression of an image in wax. But it is known that the engraving of that thing does not occur in the thing impressed; rather, its likeness occurs in it. So look at this vile doctrine – how base and contemptible it is! It is an unintelligible doctrine, and the Christians are the most base and contemptible of all sects in understanding, and the perception of realities is difficult for the likes of them.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage refutes the Christian doctrine of the incarnation (ittiḥād) of the Word (al-kalima) with Jesus. Sanusi first states the general claim: the hypostasis of knowledge (identified as the Word) united with Jesus's human nature, making him divine. Then he examines three interpretations of this union. First interpretation: the Word subsists in Jesus as an accident in a substance. This would require the Word to leave the divine essence (which is the sum of the three hypostases), but Christians deny any separation. Moreover, the same meaning cannot subsist in two distinct essences. Second interpretation: mixture, like wine and water. This is absurd because mixture is a physical property of bodies, but the Word is a non-physical meaning. Third interpretation: impression, like a seal on wax. But an impression leaves only a likeness, not the original. All three are rationally impossible. Sanusi concludes that the Christian doctrine is extremely weak and unintelligible, and Christians are the most deficient in understanding.

Key Terms
اتحاد (union)
ناسوت (human nature)
لاهوت (divine nature)
عرض (accident)
جوهر (substance)
اختلاط (mixture)
انطباع (impression)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The incarnation is a divine mystery that cannot be rationally explained.
Correction: Sanusi shows that each proposed rational explanation is self-contradictory, so the doctrine is not merely mysterious but irrational.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the problem with the interpretation that the Word united with Jesus as an accident subsists in a substance?
A) It would require the Word to become physical.
B) It would necessitate the Word's separation from the divine essence, which Christians deny.
C) It would make Jesus a mere illusion.
D) It would mean the Word ceases to exist.

Key Takeaway
The Christian doctrine of incarnation fails because none of its interpretations can withstand rational scrutiny.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study a doctrine that contradicts clear logic, do you ever stop to examine whether your own beliefs rest on assumptions you have not critically examined? What would it take for you to honestly scrutinize a belief you hold dear?
?
Sanusi describes the Christians as 'the most base and contemptible of all sects in understanding.' How does this language challenge you to value sound reasoning in your own faith? Are there areas where you cling to irrational practices out of habit or blind following?
?
This lesson shows that even sincere attempts to explain the divine can lead to absurdity without proper rational foundations. Have you ever accepted a religious teaching without demanding clear evidence? Commit to learning the rational proofs for your aqeedah before the Day when excuses will be cut off.
