وأَمَّا مَنْ قَرُبَتْ بلادُهُ مِنْ بلادِ المسلمين ، ووصلته دعوة النبيِّ صلَّى الله عليهِ وسلَّمَ على وجهها ، وأمكنته معرفتُها مِنَ المسلمينَ . . فالغزالي
( فإنهم على الأعم الأغلب لا يتأهلون للنظر ، وفي حكمهم من ذكره حجة الإسلام بقوله المنقول قريباً : ( سمعوا ضدَّ أوصافه ، وهذا لا يحرك داعية النظر والطلب ) ، وما التمسه الإمام المصنف عذراً للحجة .. هو ما عليه في الواقع ، وهو مذهب إمامه إمام الدنيا محمد بن إدريس الشافعي ، حيث ذكر في ( الأم » ( ٢٥٣/٤ ) : ( فأما من لم تبلغه دعوة المسلمين . . فلا يجوز أن يقاتلوا حتى يُدْعَوا إلى الإيمان ... ( إلى أن قال : ( ولا أعلم أحداً لم تبلغه الدعوة اليوم إلا أن يكون من وراء عدونا الذين يقاتلونا أمة من المشركين ، فلعل أولئك ألا تكون الدعوة بلغتهم ؛ وذلك مثل أن يكونوا خلف الروم أو الترك أو الخزر أمة لا نعرفهم ) ، واليوم كالأمس ؛ فترى بعض المسلمين ممن أقام في غير ديار الإسلام مَنْ يجهل المعلوم من الدين بالضرورة ؛ كتحريم شرب الخمر مثلاً ، فكيف بغير المسلمين ؟! وأقول ثانية : لا تغرَّنَّك سعة الإعلام وانتشاره ؛ إذ حاله كما قال حجة الإسلام : ( وهذا لا يحرّك داعية النظر ) ، فبلوغ الدعوة لا يعتبر إلا بهذا الشرط ، فصورة الإسلام المشوَّهة التي تستطير في الرائي والمذياع وغيرهما من وسائل الإعلام.. كيف تكون داعية لطلب الحقِّ ؟! بل هي داعية للإعراض عنه ، فلا تعجب أن ترى اليومَ مَنْ له حكم أهل الفترة هو الأعم ، ولله سبحانه رحماتٌ خَبَأَها في كل شيء ؛ حتى في الجهل ، فسبحان من وسعت رحمته كل شيء !
Prophethood; as our children [know] that a liar called al-Muqanna' falsely claimed prophethood, so these are in my view like the first category; for although they did not hear his description, they heard the opposite of his descriptions, and that does not stir the incentive for reflection and inquiry. And you see that Hujjat al-Islam (al-Ghazali) is with the majority; for whoever has not been reached by the call, or it reached him in a way that does not impel research and inquiry... he is excused before Allah Almighty, and his ruling is the ruling of the people of the interval (ahl al-fatrah). Perhaps what reached Imam al-Qadi 'Iyad is contrary to that. And the great scholar, the ocean of usul, Abu 'Abd Allah al-Zarkashi noticed the gravity of the Qadi's statement regarding Imam al-Ghazali, so he quoted his full statement in 'al-Bahr al-Muhit' (6/238) then said: 'What he attributed to al-Ghazali is a mistake against him; for he explicitly stated the corruption of the 'Anbari school as mentioned before, and he is innocent of this statement. What he referred to in his book 'al-Tafriqa' is his saying: that those Christians of Rome or Turks whom the call has not reached are excused, and that does not mean approving them. The discussion is only about those whom the call reached and they stubbornly rejected. I have only alerted to this so that the one who comes across it is not deceived.' And the author will emphasize that after finishing the quotation.
I say: What I think is that al-Ghazali, may Allah have mercy on him, mentioned in 'al-Tafriqa' the excuse on behalf of those whose lands are far from the lands of Muslims, and the call of the Prophet, peace be upon him, did not reach them at all, or it reached them in an improper manner; such as women, the mentally deficient, and the like.
As for those whose lands are near Muslim lands, and the call of the Prophet reached them properly, and they were able to know it from Muslims... al-Ghazali [said nothing about them being excused]. For in most cases they are not qualified for reflection, and in their ruling are those whom Hujjat al-Islam mentioned in his statement quoted recently: 'They heard the opposite of his descriptions, and that does not stir the incentive for reflection and inquiry.' And what the Imam, the author, sought as an excuse for the proof... is what is actually the case, and it is the school of his imam, the imam of the world, Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i, where he mentioned in 'al-Umm' (4/253): 'As for those whom the call of Muslims has not reached... it is not permissible to fight them until they are invited to faith...' until he said: 'I do not know anyone today whom the call has not reached except those who are behind our enemies who fight us, a nation of polytheists; perhaps the call has not reached them; such as those behind the Romans or the Turks or the Khazars, a nation we do not know.' And today is like yesterday; so you see some Muslims who reside in non-Muslim lands who are ignorant of what is necessarily known of the religion, such as the prohibition of drinking wine, for example; so how about non-Muslims? And I say again: Do not be deceived by the extent of media and its spread; for its condition is as Hujjat al-Islam said: 'This does not stir the incentive for reflection.' So the reaching of the call is only considered with this condition. The distorted image of Islam that spreads on television, radio, and other media... how can it be an invitation to seek the truth? Rather, it is an invitation to turn away from it. So do not be amazed that today you see that those who have the ruling of the people of the interval are the majority. And to Allah are mercies hidden in everything, even in ignorance. Glory be to Him whose mercy encompasses everything!
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on who is excused for not believing due to not receiving the call of Islam properly. The author begins by giving an example: a false prophet named al-Muqanna', where some people only heard negative descriptions of him, which does not motivate them to investigate his truth. Similarly, if someone receives a distorted image of Islam, they are not obligated to seek the truth. The author then states that al-Ghazali (Hujjat al-Islam) agrees with the majority that those who have not received the call or received it in a way that does not prompt inquiry are excused and are like the people of the interval (ahl al-fatrah)—those who lived in periods without prophets. The author notes that al-Qadi 'Iyad might have had a different understanding. Then al-Zarkashi corrects a false attribution to al-Ghazali: al-Ghazali only excused those far away like Romans and Turks if the call did not reach them properly, not those who received it and persisted in denial. The author then gives his own opinion, distinguishing between those far from Muslim lands (excused) and those near who received the call properly (not excused). He cites al-Shafi'i's view that those beyond enemy lines who have not heard the call are not to be fought until they are invited. The author extends this to modern times, arguing that even if media spreads a distorted image, it does not motivate reflection, so many people today are still like the people of the interval. The passage concludes by emphasizing Allah's mercy.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Modern media ensures that everyone has received the true call of Islam.
Correction: The distorted image of Islam in media may actually repel people and not satisfy the condition of the call prompting reflection.

Checkpoint

A) The person must hear the name 'Islam'.
B) The call must be presented in a way that stirs the incentive for reflection.
C) The person must have access to the internet.
D) The call must come from a Muslim scholar.

Key Takeaway
Today, many people are excused because the call reaches them in a distorted way that repels rather than invites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt superior to someone who is ignorant of Islam, assuming they should know better? How does this lesson's teaching about the condition of the call challenge that attitude, and what will you do today to replace judgment with mercy?
?
In what specific ways might your own conduct or speech distort the image of Islam for those around you? Commit to one concrete change this week to ensure you are an invitation to the truth, not a barrier.
?
The author says that Allah's mercy encompasses even ignorance. Does this truth fill you with hope or with fear? How should it transform the way you interact with non-Muslims or Muslims who are weak in practice?

The First Three Principles of Disbelief and Innovations
Sanusi enumerates and defines the first three principles of disbelief and innovations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first three principles of disbelief and innovations as defined by Sanusi: self-necessitation, rational approval, and blameworthy imitation.

Core Idea
Three foundational errors destroy sound belief: denying divine choice, obligating Allah, and blind partisanship.

Sanusi begins his treatment of the roots of disbelief and innovation by naming seven principles, then defines the first three. These are not merely outward sins but deep theological corruptions that undermine the Islamic understanding of Allah's will, justice, and the pursuit of truth. Understanding them is essential for recognizing and avoiding deviation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the structure: a heading, a summary statement, then three bullet-point definitions, followed by footnotes.
The footnotes provide additional context.
2
Identify each principle
For each of the three principles, note its name and its definition. Understand what error it describes.
Self-necessitation denies choice, rational approval imposes purposes, blameworthy imitation rejects truth-seeking.
3
Reflect on why these are principles of disbelief
Think about how each error leads to a distorted view of Allah and religion. Why would Sanusi list these as foundational roots?
These are not acts but underlying beliefs that generate further innovation.
4
Consider the footnote
The footnote mentions that the author elsewhere counted six principles and discussed ignorance. This shows the development of his thought.
The footnote is a scholarly reference.

وَأُصُولُ الْكُفْرِ وَالْبِدَع سَبْعَةٌ (١) :
- الإِيجَابُ الذَّاتِيُّ : وَهُوَ إِسْنَادُ الْكَائِنَاتِ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى عَلَى سَبِيلِ التَّعْلِيلِ أَوِ الطَّبْعِ مِنْ غَيْرِ اخْتِيَارِ

(۱) ذكرها المصنف في شرح العقيدة الصغرى ) ( ص ۲۸۸ ) وعدَّها ستة ، ولم يذكر الجهل بالقواعد العقلية واللغة العربية كأصل برأسه ، بل جعل ذلك سبباً وعلَّةً للأصل السادس ، وذلك قوله فيها : ( والتمسك في أصول العقائد بمجرد ظواهر الكتاب والسنة من غير عرضها على البراهين العقلية والقواطع الشرعية ؛ للجهل بأدلة العقول ، وعدم الارتياض بأساليب العرب ، والجهل بما تقرر في

بناني » ( ص ٣٤ ) .
The Fourth Introduction: On the Principles of Disbelief and Innovations. (1) And the principles of disbelief and innovations are seven: (1) Self-necessitation (al-iijāb al-dhātī): it is attributing existent things to Allah Almighty by way of causation or nature without choice. (2) Rational approval (al-taḥsīn al-ʿaqlī): it is that the acts of Allah Almighty and His rulings are rationally dependent on purposes, which are: bringing benefits and repelling harms. (3) Blameworthy imitation (al-taqlīd al-radī'): it is following others for the sake of partisanship and fanaticism without seeking the truth. (1) The author mentioned them in Sharḥ al-ʿAqīda al-Ṣughrā (p. 288) and counted them as six, and did not mention ignorance of rational principles and the Arabic language as a separate principle, but made that a cause for the sixth principle. He said there: 'Adhering in the principles of belief to the mere outward meanings of the Book and the Sunnah without presenting them to rational proofs and definitive legal evidences, due to ignorance of intellectual arguments, lack of familiarity with the styles of the Arabs, and ignorance of the rules and principles established in the art of Arabic grammar and rhetoric.' (2) Bringing benefits: such as justice and benevolence; repelling harms: such as injustice and oppression.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and definition of the first three of the seven principles of disbelief and innovations. The author begins by stating that these principles are seven, then defines each. The first is 'self-necessitation' (al-iijāb al-dhātī), which means attributing the existence of created things to Allah as a necessary causal or natural process without divine choice, thus denying volition. The second is 'rational approval' (al-taḥsīn al-ʿaqlī), the idea that Allah's actions and rulings are rationally conditional upon purposes like bringing benefits (maṣāliḥ) and repelling harms (mafāsid), implying that Allah is obligated to act for certain ends. The third is 'blameworthy imitation' (al-taqlīd al-radī'), which is following others out of partisanship (ḥamiyya) and fanaticism without sincerely seeking the truth. The footnotes provide additional context: the author elsewhere enumerated six principles, omitting ignorance as a separate principle, and clarifies that rational and linguistic ignorance underlies the sixth principle. The second footnote defines 'bringing benefits' and 'repelling harms' with examples.

Key Terms
الإيجاب الذاتي
التحسين العقلي
التقليد الرديء
جلب المصالح
درء المفاسد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think these principles are only about outward actions, but they are actually about underlying theological errors.
Correction: The principles are not actions but erroneous beliefs that form the foundation of disbelief and innovation.

Checkpoint
What does 'al-taḥsīn al-ʿaqlī' (rational approval) mean according to Sanusi?
A) Attributing existent things to Allah without choice.
B) That Allah's acts are rationally dependent on purposes like benefits and harms.
C) Following others out of fanaticism.
D) Ignorance of rational principles.

Key Takeaway
The first three principles of disbelief are theological errors: denying Allah's choice, making His acts conditional on purposes, and blind imitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking that Allah 'should' have done something differently in your life or in the world? Where does that assumption come from, and does it align with denying self-necessitation?
?
When you follow a religious opinion or practice, is it because you have truly sought the truth from the sources, or because it’s what your family, teacher, or community expects? What would it cost you to break from blind imitation?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is not obligated to bring benefits or repel harms for you, would you still trust Him? What does your reaction to hardship reveal about your heart regarding rational approval?

The Seven Roots of Disbelief and Innovation
Sanusi enumerates and defines the seven roots of disbelief and innovation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven foundational errors that lead to disbelief or innovation, as defined by Sanusi.

Core Idea
Seven roots feed the tree of misbelief: from false causality to ignorance of Arabic.

Sanusi, in this fourth introduction, identifies the key sources of theological error. These seven roots are not mere opinions but clearly defined positions that, if held, can lead to kufr or bid‘a. As you study each, notice how they share a common flaw: denial of divine freedom or misuse of reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation together. Let the author's concise definitions sink in before you analyze.
Pay attention to the structure: each root is given a technical name followed by a brief definition.
2
Identify each root and its definition
Go through the list, noting how Sanusi distinguishes each error. Write down the Arabic term and its English equivalent.
3
Understand the concluding remark
The last sentence explains the consequence of holding any of these beliefs: it may result in clear-cut disbelief or in an innovation whose status is disputed. Reflect on why Sanusi makes this distinction.
This shows that not every root leads to the same level of culpability.
4
Connect to real-world examples
For each root, think of a modern or historical example where that error led people astray. For instance, 'adhering to literal meanings' can be seen in some extreme literalist groups.
This helps internalize the concepts beyond mere memorization.

المقدمة الرابعة
في أصول الكفر والبدع
(۱)
وَأُصُولُ الْكُفْرِ وَالْبِدَع سَبْعَةٌ (١) :
- الإِيجَابُ الذَّاتِيُّ : وَهُوَ إِسْنَادُ الْكَائِنَاتِ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى عَلَى سَبِيلِ التَّعْلِيلِ أَوِ الطَّبْعِ مِنْ غَيْرِ اخْتِيَارِ
- وَالتَّحْسِينُ الْعَقْلِيُّ : وَهُوَ كَوْنُ أَفْعَالِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَأَحْكَامِهِ مَوْقُوفَةٌ عَقْلاً عَلَى الْأَغْرَاضِ ؛ وَهِيَ : جَلْبُ الْمَصَالِحِ ، وَدَرْهُ الْمَفَاسِدِ
- وَالتَّقْلِيدُ الرَّدِيءُ : وَهُوَ مُتَابَعَةُ الْغَيْرِ لأَجْلِ الْحَمِيَّةِ وَالتَّعَصُّبِ مِنْ غَيْرِ طَلَبِ لِلْحَقِّ
- وَالرَّبْطُ الْعَادِيُّ : وَهُوَ إِثْبَاتُ التَّلَازُمِ بَيْنَ أَمْرٍ وَأَمْرٍ وُجُوداً أَوْ عَدَماً بِوَاسِطَةِ التَّكَرُرِ .
- وَالْجَهْلُ الْمُرَكَّبُ : وَهُوَ أَنْ يَجْهَلَ الْحَقَّ ، وَيَجْهَلَ جَهْلَهُ بِهِ .
- وَالتَّمَسُّكُ في عَقَائِدِ الإِيمَانِ بِمُجَرَّدِ ظَوَاهِرِ الْكِتَابِ وَالسُّنَّةِ : مِنْ غَيْرِ تَفْصِيلِ بَيْنَ مَا يَسْتَحِيلُ ظَاهِرُهُ مِنْهَا وَمَا لا يَسْتَحِيلُ .
- وَالْجَهْلُ بِالْقَوَاعِدِ الْعَقْلِيَّةِ : الَّتِي هِيَ الْعِلْمُ بِوُجُوبِ الْوَاجِبَاتِ ، وَجَوَازِ الْجَائِزَاتِ ، وَاسْتِحَالَةِ الْمُسْتَحِيلاتِ ، وَبِاللِّسَانِ الْعَرَبِيِّ : الَّذِي هُوَ عِلْمُ اللُّغَةِ وَالإِعْرَابِ وَالْبَيَانِ
يعني : أنَّ اعتقاد واحدٍ مِنْ هذه الأمورِ : قد ينشأ عنه كفرٌ مُجمَعٌ عليه ، وقد ينشأ عنه بدعةٌ مُختلف في كفرِ صاحبها
The Fourth Introduction: On the Roots of Disbelief and Innovations. The roots of disbelief and innovations are seven: (1) Essential necessitation: ascribing events to Allah Almighty by way of causation or nature without choice. (2) Rational approval: that the actions of Allah Almighty and His rulings are rationally dependent on purposes, which are: bringing benefits and repelling harms. (3) Vicious imitation: following others for the sake of zealotry and fanaticism without seeking the truth. (4) Habitual connection: affirming a necessary connection between two things in existence or non-existence by means of repetition. (5) Compounded ignorance: being ignorant of the truth and being ignorant of one's ignorance of it. (6) Adhering in the doctrines of faith to mere literal meanings of the Book and the Sunnah without distinguishing between what is impossible in its literal sense and what is not impossible. (7) Ignorance of the rational principles, which are knowledge of the obligation of obligations, the permissibility of permissible things, and the impossibility of impossible things, and [ignorance] of the Arabic tongue, which is knowledge of language, grammar, and rhetoric. Meaning: that the belief in any one of these matters may result in a consensus of disbelief [in its adherent], or may result in an innovation concerning whose adherent there is disagreement as to disbelief.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and definition of the seven roots that lead to disbelief or innovation. The author begins by stating that the roots of disbelief (kufr) and innovations (bida') are seven. He then lists each root with its definition.

1. Essential necessitation (al-ījāb al-dhātī): This is the belief that God's actions are necessitated by His essence, like a cause produces its effect, without free choice. This is the root of the philosophers' denial of divine will.

2. Rational approval (al-taḥsīn al-ʿaqlī): This is the view that God's actions and rulings are subject to rational purposes, namely bringing benefits and repelling harms. This implies that God is bound to act for human interests, which is a form of anthropomorphism.

3. Vicious imitation (al-taqlīd al-radīʾ): Following others out of zealotry and bias without seeking truth, which closes the door to rational inquiry.

4. Habitual connection (al-rabṭ al-ʿādī): Affirming a necessary causal connection between two things based on repeated observation, which leads to denying God's direct creation of events.

5. Compounded ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab): Not knowing the truth and being unaware of one's ignorance, which prevents seeking knowledge.

6. Adhering to literal meanings (al-tamasuk bi-mujarrad ẓawāhir al-kitāb wa-l-sunna): Taking scripture at face value without distinguishing between verses whose apparent meaning is impossible for God and those that are not, leading to anthropomorphism.

7. Ignorance of rational principles and the Arabic tongue (al-jahl bi-l-qawāʿid al-ʿaqliyya wa-l-lisān al-ʿarabī): Lacking knowledge of the three rational judgments (obligatory, permissible, impossible) and of Arabic language, grammar, and rhetoric, which are necessary for correct interpretation.

The passage concludes that believing any of these may result in clear-cut disbelief or in an innovation whose status as disbelief is disputed among scholars.

Key Terms
أصول الكفر والبدع
الإيجاب الذاتي
التَّحْسِينُ الْعَقْلِيُّ
التَّقْلِيدُ الرَّدِيءُ
الرَّبْطُ الْعَادِيُّ
الْجَهْلُ الْمُرَكَّبُ
التَّمَسُّكُ بِظَوَاهِرِ الْكِتَابِ وَالسُّنَّةِ
الْجَهْلُ بِالْقَوَاعِدِ الْعَقْلِيَّةِ
اللِّسَانُ الْعَرَبِيُّ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that adhering to the literal meanings of the Quran and Sunnah is always praiseworthy and sufficient for correct faith.
Correction: The passage warns that without distinguishing between possible and impossible literal meanings, one can fall into error (e.g., anthropomorphism). Proper interpretation requires rational principles and Arabic knowledge.

Checkpoint
Which root is defined as 'ascribing events to Allah Almighty by way of causation or nature without choice'?
A) Rational approval
B) Essential necessitation
C) Habitual connection
D) Compounded ignorance

Key Takeaway
The seven roots of disbelief and innovation are systematic errors that undermine sound theology; awareness of them protects one's faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these seven roots have you unwittingly embraced in your own thinking about God? How has that affected your worship and trust in Him, and what step will you take this week to uproot it?
?
When you read a verse that describes God in human-like terms, do you instinctively take it literally? How does the sixth root challenge you to reexamine your interpretation, and what will you do to learn the principles that distinguish possible from impossible meanings?
?
Think of a religious practice you follow because your family or community does. Is there a chance you are engaging in vicious imitation? How can you seek the truth directly from the sources, even if it means going against tradition?

The Invalidity of Rational Good and Evil
Sanusi condemns rational good and evil as the source of kufr and deviation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the doctrine of rational good and evil leads to disbelief according to Sanusi, and how it underlies the errors of the Barahima and Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Rational good and evil is the root of both denying prophethood and obligating God.

In this passage, Sanusi turns to the second dangerous doctrine: rational good and evil. He declares that this seemingly philosophical position actually leads to clear-cut disbelief. By examining its consequences as outlined by Sanusi, we see why the Ash'ari tradition rejects the idea that reason alone can determine moral value without revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
2
Identify the doctrine
Recognize what 'al-tahsin al-'aqli' means: the claim that reason can judge good and evil independently of revelation.
3
Note the two affected groups
Sanusi explicitly mentions the Barahima (Indian philosophers) and the Mu'tazila. Observe how each group falls into error due to this doctrine.
4
Understand the consequences
The Barahima deny prophethood; the Mu'tazila obligate God to do what is best and make reason independent of revelation. These are serious deviations.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Ask yourself: Why does Sanusi consider this doctrine so dangerous? How does it undermine the necessity of revelation and proper theology?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأمرُ الثاني ؛ وهو التحسينُ العقلي : فقد نشأ عنه كفر صريح مُجمَعٌ عليه ؛ وهو كفر البراهمة ؛ فإنَّهم أنكروا (١) قال الإمام المصنف في شرح العقيدة الصغرى » ( ص ٢۸۹ ) : ( هو أصل كفر البراهمة من الفلاسفة ؛ حتى نفوا النبوات ، وأصل ضلالة المعتزلة ؛ حتى أوجبوا على الله تعالى مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح لخلقه ، وعللوا أفعاله وأحكامه بالأغراض ، وجعلوا العقل يتوصل وحده دون شرع إلى أحكام الله تعالى الشرعية ، إلى غير ذلك من الضلالات )
As for the second matter, which is rational goodness and badness (التحسين العقلي): it gave rise to clear disbelief upon which there is consensus, which is the disbelief of the Barahima; for they denied (1). The Imam, the author, said in his commentary on 'al-Aqidah al-Sughra' (p. 289): 'It is the root of the disbelief of the Barahima among the philosophers, as they even denied prophethood, and the root of the misguidance of the Mu'tazila, as they even obligated upon Allah, may He be exalted, to observe what is best and most beneficial for His creation, and they gave reasons for His actions and rulings based on purposes, and they made reason alone, without revelation, arrive at the Sharia rulings of Allah, may He be exalted, and other misguidances.'
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage addresses the second dangerous doctrine: rational good and evil (التحسين العقلي), i.e., the claim that reason can independently determine moral value apart from revelation. The author declares that this doctrine has generated clear and unanimously agreed-upon disbelief: the disbelief of the Barahima (a group of Indian philosophers). The Barahima, on the basis of rational ethics, denied the need for prophets. The author then quotes from his own commentary to explain that this doctrine is not only the root of the Barahima's kufr but also the root of the Mu'tazila's deviation, as they obligated God to do what is best (صلاح وأصلح), gave reasons for His actions (تعليل بالأغراض), and made reason independent of revelation in deriving Sharia rulings. Thus, rationalist ethics leads to rejecting revelation and misrepresenting God.

Key Terms
تحسين عقلي
تقبيح عقلي
براهمة
معتزلة
نبوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The doctrine of rational good and evil is a neutral philosophical position that does not affect core beliefs.
Correction: Sanusi demonstrates that this doctrine necessarily leads to disbelief by undermining the necessity of prophethood and misrepresenting God's attributes and actions.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the result of the doctrine of rational good and evil?
A) It leads to a better understanding of God
B) It leads to the denial of prophethood among the Barahima
C) It strengthens the role of revelation
D) It is acceptable if balanced with scripture

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of rational good and evil is not neutral; it directly leads to the denial of prophethood and to serious theological errors about God's nature and the role of revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command in the Quran or Sunnah that seems unreasonable to you, do you instinctively resist it? What does this passage teach you about the danger of relying on your own sense of good and evil?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that God should act in a certain way because it seems 'right' to you? How does Sanusi's warning against obligating God challenge that assumption?
?
In what subtle ways do you elevate your own reasoning over revelation in your daily life—perhaps in matters of worship, ethics, or theology? Are you willing to surrender that independence to the authority of the Prophet?

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Rational Good and Evil
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila claim that acts are intrinsically good or evil and shows its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's two arguments against the Mu'tazila claim that acts are intrinsically good or evil and why it implies need in God.

Core Idea
If God can cause an act, He can command it—good and evil depend on divine will, not reason.

The Mu'tazila held that certain acts are intrinsically good or evil, making God obligated to command the good and avoid the evil. Sanusi shows this undermines God's sovereignty and self-sufficiency. In this passage, he presents two concise refutations: one from God's own actions, and one from the implications of purpose in God's rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the flow of Sanusi's argument. Notice how he starts with the Mu'tazila position before refuting it.
2
Identify the two arguments
Look for two distinct refutations: the first from God's actions (He can cause postures of worship without commanding them), the second from the need for purposes (which would imply need in God).
The second argument uses the phrase 'لو توقفت أفعاله... على الأغراض' (if His actions depended on purposes).
3
Understand the consequence: Mu'tazila innovations
The passage concludes that from this corrupt principle arose Mu'tazila doctrines like requiring God to do the best for servants and making Sharia follow rational judgment.
These innovations are listed at the end: 'إيجابهم مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح' and 'كون الأحكام الشرعية تابعة لتحسين العقل وتقبيحه'.
4
Reflect on the core idea
Pause and ask: Why does Sanusi's argument from God's actions (causing a person to be in a posture of bowing) undermine the Mu'tazila claim? Connect it to the Ash'ari principle that God's will is not constrained by created notions of good and evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النبوة (١) ، وكذَّبوا الرُّسُلَ عليهم الصلاة والسلام فيما بلغوه عن المولى تبارك وتعالى ؛ مِنْ إيجاب الركوع والسجودِ ، وإباحة ذبح البهائمِ للأكل ، ونحوِ ذلك ، وهذا كله عندهم قبيح ، يستحيلُ أن يشرعَهُ الحكيم ولو تأملوا أدنى تأمُّل لعرفوا فساد رأيهم ؛ لأنَّهُ لو قَبُحَ ذلكَ في حكمه تعالى لقَبُحَ في فعله جلَّ وعلا ، ومِنَ المعلومِ قطعاً أنَّ المولى تبارك وتعالى قد يجعلُ شخصاً بمرض أو كبر على هيئة الراكع أو على هيئة الساجدِ ، بل قد يَسْلُبُ عقلَهُ حتى يصدرَ منه ما هو أعظمُ مِنْ هذا ؛ مِنْ كشف العورة ، وأكلِ العَذِرَةِ وسائرِ النجاساتِ ، والتلطُّخِ بها ! فإذا كانَ لهُ تعالى أنْ يفعل ما يشاءُ .. فله جلَّ وعلا أن يحكم في عبيده بما يشاء ولو توقفَتْ أفعاله سبحانَهُ وأحكامه على الأغراض.. لزم احتياجه تعالى إلى الأفعالِ ليُحصِّلَ بها غرضَهُ ، وذلك ينافي جلاله وعظمته ووجوبَ غناه جلَّ وعلا عن كل ما سواه تعالى ونشأ عن هذا الأصل الفاسد بدعة المعتزلة : في إيجابهم مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح للعبادِ في حقِّهِ تعالى ، وكون الأحكام الشرعية تابعة لتحسين العقل وتقبيحِهِ ، ونحو ذلكَ مِنْ بدعهم
They denied prophethood, and they belied the Messengers, peace be upon them, regarding what they conveyed from the Lord, Blessed and Exalted, such as the obligation of bowing and prostration, and the permissibility of slaughtering animals for eating, and the like. All of this, according to them, is evil (qabīḥ), impossible for the Wise to legislate. And if they had reflected even the slightest reflection, they would have known the corruption of their opinion; because if that were evil in His ruling, then it would be evil in His action, Glorified and Exalted. And it is known with certainty that the Lord, Blessed and Exalted, may cause a person, due to illness or old age, to be in the posture of the one bowing or in the posture of the one prostrating; rather, He may even deprive him of his mind so that he does something greater than this: uncovering the private parts, eating filth and all impurities, and smearing himself with them! So if He, Exalted, can do whatever He wills, then He, Glorious and Exalted, can rule over His servants as He wills. And if His actions, Glorified is He, and His rulings were dependent on purposes, then He would need actions to attain His purpose, and that contradicts His majesty and greatness and the necessity of His self-sufficiency, Glorious and Exalted, from everything other than Him. And from this corrupt principle arose the innovation of the Mu'tazila: their requiring consideration of what is good and best (al-ṣalāḥ wa al-aṣlaḥ) for the servants in His right, and that the legal rulings follow the rational determination of good and evil (taḥsīn al-ʿaql wa taqbīḥihi), and the like of their innovations.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila claim that certain acts are intrinsically good (ḥasan) or evil (qabīḥ) independent of divine command, and that God must command the good and avoid the evil. The author begins by stating the Mu'tazila position: they deny prophethood (or rather, they deny the need for prophets? the text says they denied the prophets' message regarding the obligation of bowing and prostration, etc.) and consider such obligations and permissions to be 'evil' (qabīḥ), impossible for the Wise to legislate. Then the refutation: if such acts were evil in God's command, they would also be evil in His action. But it is known that God can cause a person, through illness or old age, to be in the posture of bowing or prostration without it being worship; indeed, He can cause madness leading to even worse acts like uncovering private parts or eating filth. Since God can do such things, He can also command them. The second argument: if God's actions and rulings were based on purposes, that would imply need in God, which contradicts His self-sufficiency. Finally, the author notes that from this erroneous principle arose the Mu'tazila innovation of requiring God to do the 'best' for servants (al-ṣalāḥ wa al-aṣlaḥ) and making Sharia dependent on rational judgment.

Key Terms
قبيح
حسن
الصلاح والأصلح
تحسين العقل وتقبيحه
إيجاب
إباحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believed that acts are intrinsically good or evil, and that God must command good and avoid evil as a matter of rational necessity.
Correction: Sanusi argues that if an act were intrinsically evil, God would not cause it; but He does, so acts are not intrinsically good or evil. Good and evil depend on divine command.

Checkpoint
According to the author's refutation, what does the fact that God can cause a person to be in a posture of prostration without commanding it prove?
A) That prostration is intrinsically good.
B) That prostration is not intrinsically evil, contradicting the Mu'tazila claim.
C) That God cannot command prostration.
D) That the Mu'tazila are correct.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila by showing that God's actions are not constrained by human notions of good and evil, and that attributing purposes to God's rulings implies need, contradicting His self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a command of Allah that your mind finds difficult or 'unreasonable,' do you pause and submit, or do you internally question God's wisdom? What does your reaction reveal about your heart's alignment with Sanusi's refutation?
?
Sanusi shows that the Mu'tazila effectively denied prophethood because they rejected commands they deemed 'evil.' Are there any prophetic commands or divine decrees in your life that you struggle to accept as good? Will you repent for that resistance and ask for surrender?
?
If God's actions are not dependent on any purpose outside Himself, then what comfort does this give you when you face trials that seem purposeless? Can you truly worship a God whose ways you may not understand, trusting that His sovereignty is absolute?

Refuting the Mu'tazila: The Lām in 'Li-Ya'budūn' as Metaphor
Sanusi refutes Mu'tazila by explaining the lām as a metaphorical purpose via tābi'iyya metaphor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the lām in 'li-ya'budūn' is a metaphorical rather than real indication of purpose, and how the tābi'iyya metaphor refutes the Mu'tazila claim.

Core Idea
The lām of purpose in the Quran is metaphorical, not real.

The Mu'tazila argued that Allah creates for a purpose based on the verse 'wa mā khalaqtu... illā li-ya'budūn'. However, Sanusi shows this is a rhetorical error. The verse uses a derivative metaphor (isti'āra tābi'iyya) where obligation is likened to a final cause. The lām follows that metaphor, indicating intended result, not necessary causation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila literally interpret the lām in 'li-ya'budūn' as indicating real purpose, implying worship is the actual goal of creation.
This leads to theological problems if worship doesn't occur.
2
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read Sanusi's refutation carefully, noting his use of rhetorical terms.
3
Identify key rhetorical concepts
Learn the meanings of 'tābi'iyya metaphor' and 'real vs metaphorical purpose' as described by Sanusi.
These terms are essential for understanding the argument.
4
Apply the pen analogy
Sanusi's example of sharpening a pen for writing shows that the intended purpose may not always be fulfilled; similarly, creation does not necessitate worship.
This clarifies why the lām is metaphorical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الجهل بفنّ علم المعاني والبيانِ : أخذُ المعتزلة تعليل أفعال المولى تبارك وتعالى بالأغراض مِنْ قولِهِ جلَّ وعلا : ﴿ وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ ﴾ [الذاريات : ٥٦] ، فجعلوا اللام للتعليل حقيقة ، ولو خالطوا فنَّ البيانِ لعرفوا أنَّ الآيةَ مِنْ بابِ الاستعارة التبعيَّةِ ، وأَنَّهُ شُبِّهَ التكليف بالعبادة في ترتُّبِهِ على الخلْقِ بالعلَّةِ الغائيَّةِ التي تترتب على الفعلِ ويُقصد الفعل لأجلها ، فجُعِلَتِ العبادة ـ أي : التكليف بها - لأجل هذا الشَّبَهِ علَّةً غائيةً بطريق الاستعارة(۲) ، فتبع ذلك استعارة اللام الموضوعة للتعليل ، ودخلَتْ في العبادةِ للدَّلالة على العلَّةِ المجازية . (۲) كقولك: هذا القلم بريته للكتابة، ثم قد تكتب به وقد لا تكتب، فالتكليف بالعبادة يتصور بعده وقوعها وعدمُهُ، بخلاف ما لو كان الخلق لأجل العبادة حقيقة؛ فحينئذ لا يتصور التخلُّف، ولهذا المعنى صُرفتِ اللام من التعليل الحقيقي إلى التعليل المجازي تبعاً لمعنى التكليف المفاد من اللفظ المشتق ( يعبدون )
And from ignorance of the art of semantics (ma‘ānī) and rhetoric (bayān): the Mu‘tazila taking the causation of the actions of the Master, blessed and exalted, by purposes from His statement, glorified and exalted: 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except that they might worship Me' [adh-Dhariyat 51:56]. They made the lām for real purpose (ta‘līl ḥaqīqī). If they had mixed in the art of rhetoric, they would know that the verse is from the category of tābi‘iyya (derivative) metaphor, and that obligation (taklīf) is compared to worship in its result from creation to the final cause (al-‘illa al-ghā’iyya) which results from the act and for which the act is intended. So worship—i.e., the obligation of it—was made, because of this similarity, a final cause by way of metaphor (isti‘āra) (2). Then the metaphor of the lām placed for real purpose followed, and it entered into worship to indicate the metaphorical cause. (2) As your saying: 'I sharpened this pen for writing,' then you may write with it or not. So the obligation of worship can be conceived with its occurrence or non-occurrence afterwards. Unlike if the creation were truly for worship; then non-occurrence would be inconceivable. For this reason the lām was diverted from real purpose to metaphorical purpose, following the meaning of obligation derived from the derived word (yu‘budūn).
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila's claim that Allah's actions are motivated by purposes or goals, based on the verse 'wa mā khalaqtu al-jinna wa al-insa illā li-ya‘budūn'. The Mu'tazila interpreted the lām (li-) as indicating real purpose, meaning that Allah created them for the purpose of worship. The author argues that this is ignorance of rhetoric (bayān). The verse is actually a tābi‘iyya metaphor, where the relationship of creation to worship is compared to the relationship of an act to its final cause, but only metaphorically. The obligation (taklīf) is likened to a final cause. The lām is therefore used metaphorically, not literally. The footnote provides an analogy: sharpening a pen for writing does not guarantee writing; similarly, creation does not guarantee worship. The real purpose interpretation would imply that if worship does not occur, creation would be futile, which is not the case. Thus, the lām indicates intended result, not necessary causation.

Key Terms
علم المعاني
الاستعارة التبعية
العلة الغائية
التعليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that the lām in 'li-ya'budūn' indicates a real, necessary purpose for creation, meaning that worship must occur.
Correction: The lām is metaphorical; it indicates an intended outcome, not a necessary one, as shown by the analogy of the pen.

Checkpoint
What type of metaphor does the author say is used in the verse 'وما خلقت الجن والإنس إلا ليعبدون'?
A) Explicit metaphor
B) Derivative metaphor (tābi‘iyya)
C) Metonymy
D) Synecdoche

Key Takeaway
The lām in 'li-ya'budūn' does not prove that worship is the real purpose of creation; it is a metaphorical expression of obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because God created you for worship, your life automatically becomes worshipful? What does this lesson reveal about the role of your own effort and choice in making worship happen?
?
The pen analogy shows that even if you were created for worship, you may fail to actually worship. Which area of your daily routine have you treated as if God's purpose guarantees the outcome, rather than requiring your active response?
?
If the lām is metaphorical, then obligation does not force occurrence. What concrete change will you make today to 'sharpen' yourself for worship, rather than expecting it to happen automatically?

Refutation of the Hashwiyya on Divine Speech
Sanusi refutes the Hashwiyya who claim speech is letters and sounds subsisting in Allah's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine speech cannot consist of letters and sounds, as argued by Imam Sanusi against the Hashwiyya.

Core Idea
Letters and sounds are temporal by nature; the eternal cannot be temporal.

Sanusi addresses the view of the Hashwiyya who claim that Allah's speech is composed of letters and sounds subsisting in His essence, and that these letters are eternal. He refutes this by pointing out the inherent temporality of letters and sounds: they come into existence after non-existence and cease to exist. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the Hashwiyya claim and Sanusi's objection.
2
Identify the Hashwiyya claim
What do the Hashwiyya assert about divine speech? Write down their two claims: that speech is letters/sounds in Allah's essence, and that these are eternal.
They also claim ink is temporal but the written Qur'an becomes eternal.
3
Analyze Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi argues that letters and sounds are only conceivable as temporal (hadith) because they are preceded and succeeded by non-existence. Therefore, they cannot be eternal (qadim). Understand why this refutes the Hashwiyya.
The eternal cannot have non-existence before or after.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فذهب الحشويَّةُ : إلى أنَّ هذا الكلام الذي يتّصفُ بهِ مولانا جلَّ وعز حروف وأصوات قائمة بذاتِهِ على حسب ما ثبت في الكلام اللساني في الشاهد ، وزعموا أنَّهُ معَ كونه حرفاً وصوتاً قديم ، بل زعموا أنَّ المدادَ حادث ، فإذا كُتِبَ بهِ القرآنُ صارَ بعينِهِ قديماً ! وهذا المذهب واضحُ الفسادِ ؛ إِذْ مِنَ المعلومِ أنَّ الحروف والأصوات لا تُعقَلُ إلا حادثةً ؛ لتجدُّدِها بعد عدم ، وعدمها بعد تجدد ، فالعدمُ يكتنفها سابقاً ولاحقاً ، والقديم لا يقبلُ العدمَ لا سابقاً ولا لاحقاً .
So the Hashwiyya went: that this speech with which our Lord, Majestic and High, is described is letters and sounds subsisting in His essence, according to what is established of laryngeal speech in the witnessed realm. And they claimed that it, despite being a letter and sound, is eternal. Rather, they claimed that the ink is temporal, but when the Qur'an is written with it, it becomes precisely eternal! This doctrine is clearly corrupt, for it is known that letters and sounds are only conceivable as temporal, because they come into existence after non-existence, and they cease after existence. So non-existence encompasses them before and after. And the eternal does not admit non-existence either before or after.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the Hashwiyya sect on divine speech. They assert that Allah's speech consists of letters and sounds that subsist in His essence, similar to human speech, but they claim these are eternal. They go further to claim that the written ink of the Qur'an is temporal, yet the written words become eternal. Sanusi refutes this by noting that letters and sounds are inherently temporal: they come into existence after not existing and cease to exist. Non-existence encompasses them from both sides, whereas the eternal cannot be preceded or succeeded by non-existence.

Key Terms
الحشويَّة
حروف وأصوات
قديم
حادث
المداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine speech can consist of eternal letters and sounds.
Correction: Letters and sounds are inherently temporal (hadith) because they are preceded and succeeded by non-existence, so they cannot be eternal (qadim).

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi consider the Hashwiyya view corrupt?
A) Because the Qur'an is created
B) Because letters and sounds are temporal and cannot be eternal
C) Because speech is an act
D) Because Allah does not speak

Key Takeaway
Letters and sounds are temporal by nature, so they cannot be attributes of the eternal Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you ever unconsciously think of the letters as eternal, like Allah Himself? How does the knowledge that these letters are temporal affect your reverence for the recited words? Today, consciously remind yourself that the Qur'an you recite is a created recitation pointing to the uncreated speech of Allah—let that heighten your awe.
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah's attributes as similar to human attributes, even in a subtle way? In your own worship, do you treat Allah's speech as if it were like human speech? Examine your heart: is there any trace of that assumption? Repent and ask Allah to purify your tawḥīd from any likeness to creation.
?
The Hashwiyya argued that the ink is temporal but the written Qur'an becomes eternal—a confusion between the physical book and the divine speech. How do you view the mushaf in your home? Do you sometimes treat it as if it were itself eternal, forgetting that it is a created object? Resolve to honor the physical mushaf while firmly distinguishing it from the eternal attribute of Allah's speech.

Sanusi's Refutation of the Mu'tazila on Divine Speech
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila on divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila position on divine speech and why Sanusi argues it undermines the reality of Allah being a speaker.

Core Idea
Making Allah's speech a created act strips it from His essence and makes real speech impossible.

The Mu'tazila attempted to protect divine transcendence by denying that speech subsists in Allah. However, Sanusi shows that this move actually destroys the very meaning of divine speech, reducing it to a mere created phenomenon. Let's examine their claim and his response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from the Mu'tazila claim to Sanusi's refutation.
The passage ends mid-sentence at the page break, but the refutation's two arguments are clearly stated.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila say divine speech is a created act like providing or giving, created in a body and made audible. Find the key phrases: 'فعل من أفعاله' and 'خلق ذلك في جرم من الأجرام'.
3
Understand their reasoning
They argue speech cannot subsist in Allah because temporal accidents cannot subsist in Him. Note the phrase 'استحالة قيام الحوادث به'.
4
Examine the two refutation arguments
Sanusi says the view is 'واضح الفساد' for two reasons. Find both: (1) it makes speech impossible for the one who knows, and (2) if no command/prohibition is in the essence, then speech is not truly from Him.
The second argument is introduced with 'وأيضاً'.
5
Reflect on the consequences
If speech is only a created thing, then Allah does not really speak. Consider why that matters for divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبت المعتزلة : إلى أنَّ كلامه تعالى حروف وأصوات كما قالَتِ الحشويَّةُ ، إلا أنَّهم خالفوهم بأن قالوا : إِنَّ كلامَهُ تعالى فعلٌ مِنْ أفعالِهِ ؛ كرَزْقِهِ وعطائِهِ ، فلا يصح أن يقوم بذاتِهِ تعالى ؛ لاستحالة قيام الحوادثِ بهِ ، فإذا أراد الله تعالى أن يتكلم بأمرٍ أو نهي أو غيرهما مِنْ سائر أنواع الكلام .. خلقَ ذلكَ في جِرْمٍ مِنَ الأجرام ، وأسمع ذلكَ مَنْ شاءَ مِنْ ملائكته وأنبيائه ورُسُلِهِ . وهذا المذهب أيضاً واضح الفسادِ ؛ لأنَّهُ يستلزم امتناع ما عُلِمَتْ صحتُهُ مِنَ الكلام في حق العالِمِ ، وأيضاً : إذا لم يكن في الذاتِ العليَّةِ أمر ولا نهي ، ولا وعد ولا وعيد، وإنَّما هي موجودةٌ في الأجرام
And the Mu'tazila went: that His speech, Exalted, is letters and sounds as the Hashwiyya said, except they differed from them by saying: His speech, Exalted, is an act from His acts, like His provision and giving. So it is not valid that it subsists in His essence, Exalted, because of the impossibility of temporal accidents subsisting in Him. So when Allah, Exalted, intends to speak a command, prohibition, or other types of speech, He creates that in some body, and makes it heard to whomever He wills of His angels, prophets, and messengers. This doctrine is also clearly corrupt, because it necessitates the impossibility of what is known to be valid of speech for the one who knows. Moreover, if there is no command or prohibition in the exalted essence, no promise or threat, but they exist in bodies...
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila view on divine speech. They agree with the Hashwiyya that speech is letters and sounds, but they differ by saying that speech is an act of Allah, created in a body, not subsisting in His essence, because temporal accidents cannot subsist in Allah. So Allah creates speech in a body and makes it heard. Sanusi refutes this: first, it renders speech impossible for the one who knows (i.e., it would mean Allah does not really speak). Second, if there is no command, prohibition, promise, or threat in the divine essence itself, but only in created bodies, then Allah would not truly be a speaker.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
فعل من أفعاله
جرم من الأجرام
استحالة قيام الحوادث به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Mu'tazila preserve divine transcendence by making speech a created act.
Correction: Sanusi shows this actually negates divine speech, as it would remove speech from Allah's essence.

Checkpoint
What is the main error of the Mu'tazila according to Sanusi?
A) They deny Allah's existence
B) They make speech a created act, implying Allah does not truly speak
C) They affirm letters and sounds are eternal
D) They deny the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech must be an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, not a created act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you truly believe you are encountering the eternal speech of Allah that subsists in His essence, or do you treat it as a mere event from the past? How does your conviction about this affect your reverence and attentiveness in recitation?
?
If you were to imagine Allah’s speech as something created and separate from Him, how would that change your sense of His personal closeness to you when He commands, prohibits, promises, and warns you directly in the Qur'an?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed to protect Allah’s transcendence, but Sanusi shows they actually negate His speech. In your own life, are there ways you try to 'protect' Allah's majesty that actually distance you from a real relationship with Him? Will you repent of any such tendency today?

Sects and Disagreements

Third Division: Reports Necessarily False by Additional Evidence
Sanusi defines the third division of reports with Mu'tazilite examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi classifies reports that are possible in themselves but become necessarily false when weighed against broader doctrinal evidence, using Mu'tazilite claims as a concrete example.

Core Idea
A report can be linguistically possible but false when judged by established proofs.

Sanusi continues his classification of reports by introducing a third category: those that are neither certainly true nor certainly false in themselves, but become false once we consider the broader theological evidence. He illustrates this with Mu'tazilite claims about God's will, human power, and divine purposes. This passage shows the importance of contextual evidence in determining the truth value of theological statements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation once to get the overall meaning. Notice that Sanusi defines a third division and then gives an example.
The passage is cut off at the end; the argument continues, but the main point is clear.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claims
List the three specific claims Sanusi attributes to the Mu'tazila: (1) God's eternal will does not pertain to disbelief or sins, (2) originated human power is effective in actions according to human will, (3) God's acts follow purposes.
3
Understand the criterion
Notice that Sanusi says these claims are possible in their linguistic form. They become necessarily false only when we look at 'additional evidence'—specifically, proofs for the generality of God's will.
This is the key insight: a statement's truth value can change when its context is expanded.
4
Reflect on the application
Ask yourself: Why does Sanusi emphasize that falsehood is necessary only after considering proofs? This shows that kalam arguments rely on established theological principles to judge new statements.
This connects to the broader method of Ahl al-Sunna in refuting deviant views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالثُ : ما يحتمل الصدق والكذبَ بالنظر إلى ذاتِهِ وصورته فقط ، وإذا نظرنا إلى زائدِ على ذلكَ تحتّم كذبُهُ ، وارتفع احتمال الصدق
ومثال ذلكَ : قول المعتزلة : الإرادة الأزلية لا تتعلق بالكفر ولا بالمعاصي، وإنَّما تتعلق بالخير فقط ، والقدرة الحادثة المؤثرةُ في أفعال العبادِ على وَفْقِ إرادتهم ، وأفعالُ اللهِ تعالى وأحكامُهُ تتبع الأغراض ، ونحو ذلكَ مِنْ عقائدهم الفاسدة
فهذه أخبار تحتمل الصدق والكذبَ إذا قصرنا النظر على مجرَّدِ حقائقها اللغويَّةِ ، أمَّا إذا نظرنا إلى براهين عموم إرادة الله تعالى وعموم
The third division: what is possible of truth and falsehood with respect to its essence and form only, and when we look at what is additional to that, its falsehood becomes necessary and the possibility of truth is removed. And an example of that is the saying of the Mu'tazila: The eternal will (al-irada al-azaliyya) does not pertain to disbelief nor to sins, but only pertains to good; and the originated power (al-qudra al-haditha) is effective in the acts of servants in accordance with their will; and the acts of God Most High and His judgments follow purposes (atba' al-agharad); and similar to that from their corrupt beliefs. For these reports are possible of truth and falsehood if we confine our view to their mere linguistic realities, but if we look at the proofs of the generality of God's will (umum irada Allah ta'ala) and the generality...
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage defines the third division of reports: those that are possible in themselves but become necessarily false when additional evidence is considered. The example given is the false doctrines of the Mu'tazila: that God's eternal will does not apply to disbelief or sins, that human power (qudra haditha) is effective independently, and that God's acts follow purposes. In their mere linguistic form, these statements could be true or false. However, when we look at the proofs for the generality of God's will (umum irada) and other evidences, their falsehood becomes necessary. The passage ends mid-sentence at 'وعموم', suggesting the argument continues on the next page, but it clearly identifies the criterion: additional evidence (here, proofs against Mu'tazili positions) forces the judgment of falsehood.

Key Terms
قسم ثالث (third division)
إرادة أزلية (eternal will)
قدرة حادثة (originated power)
اتباع الأغراض (following purposes)
براهين (proofs)
عموم إرادة الله (generality of God's will)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazilite doctrines are obviously false by themselves, just from their wording.
Correction: Sanusi argues these statements are possible in their linguistic form; they become necessarily false only when contrasted with proofs of God's general will and other established evidences.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what makes the Mu'tazilite doctrines necessarily false?
A) Their linguistic form is contradictory
B) The additional proofs against them, like the generality of God's will
C) The fact that they are reported from the Mu'tazila
D) They are impossible by definition

Key Takeaway
A report may be linguistically possible yet become necessarily false when additional doctrinal evidence is considered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Mu'tazilite claim that God's will only pertains to good and not to sin, does it appeal to you because it makes God seem more just or less responsible for evil? Are you willing to submit to the proofs of God's general will even if it challenges your own sense of fairness? What would change in your life if you truly accepted that God wills everything, including your own sins?
?
Have you ever dismissed a teaching because it seemed possible to you linguistically or logically, without seeking out the broader proofs from scripture and reason? What is one belief you hold that you have never tested against the established proofs? What is at stake for you in re-examining it?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed that human power is effective in actions. Do you ever act as if your success or failure is entirely your own doing, forgetting that God is the ultimate creator of all actions? How would your humility and reliance on God increase if you internalized that your power is created and secondary?
